The First Ash'arite Argument: Lying as a Deficiency
First Ash'arite argument for divine truthfulness of internal speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first Ash'arite argument against divine lying and why it only establishes the truthfulness of internal speech (al-kalām al-nafsī).

Core Idea
Lying is a deficiency, but only in God's essential attribute, not in action.

This passage presents the first of three arguments for why lying is impossible for God. The argument is based on deficiency (naqṣ), but a careful analysis shows it only applies to God's internal speech, not to the words He creates. We'll explore the argument, an objection, and the response.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation to get the overall flow.
Notice the structure: main argument, objection, response.
2
Identify the main claim
Find where the author states that lying is deficiency and deficiency is impossible for God. Note the consensus reference.
3
Notice the limitation
The passage explicitly says this argument only proves the truthfulness of internal speech (kalām nafsī), not verbal speech. Underline this point.
This is crucial for the broader discussion.
4
Analyse the objection about Noah
Consider the objection: not sending Noah seems to imply possible deficiency. Follow the response that distinguishes between the possibility of non-sending and its combination with the statement 'We sent Noah'.
The response relies on the principle of choice (ikhtiyār).
5
Reflect on the significance
Think about why this argument alone is insufficient to prove truthfulness of verbal speech, and how this sets up the need for further arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما امتناع الكذب عليه ( عندنا فثلاثة أوجه : الأول. أنه نقص والنقص على الله تعالى (محال إجماعاً، وأيضاً فيلزم) على تقدير أن يقع الكذب في كلامه أن تكون ( نحن ) أكمل منه في بعض الأوقات ) أعني وقت صدقنا في كلامنا، وهذا الوجه إنما يدل على أن الكلام النفسي لدي هو صفة قائمة بذاته يكون صادقاً، وإلا لزم النقصان في صفته تعالى مع كمال صفتنا ولا يدل على صدقه في الحروف والكلمات التي يخلقها في جسم دالة على معان مقصودة، قوله : ( والنقص على الله تعالى محال إجماعاً فإن قلت : لا شك أن عدم إرساله تعالى نوحاً عليه السلام إلى قومه ممكن لأن الإرسال غير واجب عليه تعالى بل واقع باختياره، والإنكار مكابرة فيلزم إمكان النقص قلت : إمكان النقص إنما يلزم إذا أمكن اجتماع عدم الإرسال مع صدور قوله تعالى : إنا أرسلنا نوحا إلى قومه ﴾ [نوح: ١]، وهو ممنوع، وامتناع هذا الاجتماع لا ينافي إمكان عدم الإرسال في نفسه بناء على قاعدة الاختيار فتدبر. قوله : (أشار إلى دفعه بقوله : واعلم إلخ) لا يخفى أن ظاهر قول المصنف واعلم إلخ هو الاعتراض على الوجه الأول المذكور في المتن ولما توجه عليه أن اللازم من الكذب النقص من جهة صفته الذاتية لا النقص في الفعل، والمتنازع في جواز الثاني لا الأول فإنه ممتنع بالإجماع كما ستعرف تكلف الشارح في توجيه كلامه بتقدير سؤال بأن الدليل المذكور لا يدل على امتناع الكذب في كلامه اللفظي، وجواب بأنه يدل عليه أيضاً لأن خلق الكاذب نقص في فعله وجعل هذا الكلام اعتراضاً على الجواب المقدر، وأنت خبير بأن هذا لا يخلو عن تمحل، لكن دفعه بقوله : ( واعلم أنه لم يظهر لي فرق بين النقص في الفعل وبين القبح العقلي ) فيه ) فإن النقص في الأفعال هو القبح العقلي بعينه ) فيها ( وإنما تختلف العبارة) دون المعنى، فأصحابنا المنكرون للقبح العقلي كيف يتمسكون في دفع الكذب عن الكلام اللفظي بلزوم النقص في أفعاله تعالى
As for the impossibility of lying for Him (according to us, it is) by three aspects: The first: that it is deficiency (naqṣ), and deficiency upon God is impossible by consensus, and also it would follow, on the assumption that lying occurs in His speech, that we are more perfect than Him at some times, namely when we are truthful in our speech. This aspect only indicates that the internal speech (al-kalām al-nafsī) is an attribute subsisting in His essence which is truthful; otherwise a deficiency in His attribute would follow with the perfection of our attribute, and it does not indicate His truthfulness in the letters and words which He creates in a body indicating intended meanings. His saying: (And deficiency upon God is impossible by consensus). For if you say: there is no doubt that His not sending Noah, peace be upon him, to his people is possible because sending is not obligatory upon Him, rather it occurs by His choice, and denial is obstinacy, so the possibility of deficiency follows. I say: the possibility of deficiency only follows if it is possible to combine the non-sending with the utterance of His saying 'Indeed We sent Noah to his people' [71:1], which is prevented. And the impossibility of this combination does not negate the possibility of non-sending in itself, based on the principle of choice, so ponder. His saying: (He alluded to its refutation by his saying 'And know' etc.) It is not hidden that the apparent meaning of the author's saying 'And know' etc. is the objection to the first aspect mentioned in the text. And since it was directed against him that what necessarily follows from lying is deficiency in terms of His essential attribute, not deficiency in action, and the point of contention is the permissibility of the second, not the first, for it is impossible by consensus, as you will know. The commentator strained in directing his speech, by supposing a question that the mentioned proof does not indicate the impossibility of lying in His verbal speech, and an answer that it also indicates it because creating a liar is a deficiency in His action, and he made this speech an objection to the supposed answer. And you are aware that this is not free from contrivance. But he refuted it by his saying: (And know that it did not appear to me a difference between deficiency in action and rational ugliness (al-qubḥ al-ʿaqlī)). For deficiency in actions is exactly rational ugliness; only the expression differs, not the meaning. So how do our associates who deny rational ugliness argue in refuting lying from verbal speech by the necessity of deficiency in His actions?
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the first Ash'arite argument against divine lying, along with objections and responses. The first argument states that lying is a deficiency (naqṣ), and deficiency is impossible for God. Moreover, if lying occurred, humans would be more perfect when truthful, which is impossible. However, the author notes that this argument only proves the truthfulness of internal speech (kalām nafsī), not external speech (lafẓī). A commentary then raises an objection: consider God not sending Noah; that seems to imply possible deficiency. The response distinguishes between the possibility of non-sending in itself and its combination with the statement 'We sent Noah'; the latter is impossible. Another objection questions whether the argument applies to verbal speech, since creating a lie is a deficiency in action. The commentator argues that the author's statement 'And know' addresses this: there is no difference between deficiency in action and rational ugliness; they are the same. Since Ash'arites deny rational ugliness, they cannot use deficiency in action as an argument against lying in verbal speech. Thus, the argument is refined to apply only to internal speech.

Key Terms
النقص
الكلام النفسي
الكلام اللفظي
الإرسال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument proves God cannot lie in any form of speech, including created words.
Correction: The argument explicitly limits itself to internal speech; external verbal speech is not proven truthful by this argument.

Checkpoint
According to the first Ash'arite argument, what does the impossibility of lying primarily prove?
A) That God cannot lie in verbal speech.
B) That internal speech must be truthful.
C) That deficiency is possible for God.
D) That rational ugliness is the same as deficiency.

Key Takeaway
The first Ash'arite argument establishes God's truthfulness in internal speech but leaves open the possibility of lying in created verbal speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God’s internal speech is perfectly truthful, yet He can create deceptive external words, do you rely on God’s promises and warnings with absolute certainty? When have you doubted His truthfulness due to your own circumstances? How can you train your heart to trust His internal speech over your perceptions?
?
The argument shows that deficiency is impossible for God, but you are not God. Where in your life do you accept or even celebrate your own deficiencies—like lying to avoid discomfort? What would it cost you to commit to truthfulness in your speech, even when it makes you vulnerable?
?
You, like the objector in the passage, might think God’s actions could be deficient. Pause: Have you ever accused God of being unfair or lacking wisdom because of something He did or did not do? How does the distinction between internal speech and external actions help you submit to His decrees? What step can you take today to silence that accusation?

Deficiency in Action vs. Rational Ugliness
Debate over identity of deficiency in action and rational ugliness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author equates deficiency in action with rational ugliness and how this challenges the Ash'arite position on divine lying.

Core Idea
Deficiency in action and rational ugliness are identical, undermining Ash'arite reliance on deficiency.

In this passage, the commentator discusses a subtle point: is deficiency in action distinct from rational ugliness? The author argues they are identical, which creates a problem for Ash'arites who deny rational ugliness yet use deficiency to argue against divine lying. We'll see how this critique unfolds and why it matters for the consistency of the Ash'arite position.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully, noting the shift in focus from the earlier argument.
This part is more meta-theological.
2
Identify the problem
The commentator notes that the author's statement 'And know' appears to be an objection to the first argument. Understand the issue: the argument only addresses deficiency in essential attribute, not deficiency in action.
3
Understand the commentator's attempt
The commentator strains to interpret the author's words as a response to a supposed question about verbal speech, but the text suggests a different reading.
The author later clarifies the real point.
4
Grasp the author's claim
The author says: 'I do not see a difference between deficiency in action and rational ugliness; they are exactly the same.' This is the key claim.
5
See the consequence
If deficiency in action equals rational ugliness, then Ash'arites who deny rational ugliness cannot use deficiency in action to argue against verbal lying. This reveals an inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما امتناع الكذب عليه ( عندنا فثلاثة أوجه : الأول. أنه نقص والنقص على الله تعالى (محال إجماعاً، وأيضاً فيلزم) على تقدير أن يقع الكذب في كلامه أن تكون ( نحن ) أكمل منه في بعض الأوقات ) أعني وقت صدقنا في كلامنا، وهذا الوجه إنما يدل على أن الكلام النفسي لدي هو صفة قائمة بذاته يكون صادقاً، وإلا لزم النقصان في صفته تعالى مع كمال صفتنا ولا يدل على صدقه في الحروف والكلمات التي يخلقها في جسم دالة على معان مقصودة، قوله : ( والنقص على الله تعالى محال إجماعاً فإن قلت : لا شك أن عدم إرساله تعالى نوحاً عليه السلام إلى قومه ممكن لأن الإرسال غير واجب عليه تعالى بل واقع باختياره، والإنكار مكابرة فيلزم إمكان النقص قلت : إمكان النقص إنما يلزم إذا أمكن اجتماع عدم الإرسال مع صدور قوله تعالى : إنا أرسلنا نوحا إلى قومه ﴾ [نوح: ١]، وهو ممنوع، وامتناع هذا الاجتماع لا ينافي إمكان عدم الإرسال في نفسه بناء على قاعدة الاختيار فتدبر. قوله : (أشار إلى دفعه بقوله : واعلم إلخ) لا يخفى أن ظاهر قول المصنف واعلم إلخ هو الاعتراض على الوجه الأول المذكور في المتن ولما توجه عليه أن اللازم من الكذب النقص من جهة صفته الذاتية لا النقص في الفعل، والمتنازع في جواز الثاني لا الأول فإنه ممتنع بالإجماع كما ستعرف تكلف الشارح في توجيه كلامه بتقدير سؤال بأن الدليل المذكور لا يدل على امتناع الكذب في كلامه اللفظي، وجواب بأنه يدل عليه أيضاً لأن خلق الكاذب نقص في فعله وجعل هذا الكلام اعتراضاً على الجواب المقدر، وأنت خبير بأن هذا لا يخلو عن تمحل، لكن دفعه بقوله : ( واعلم أنه لم يظهر لي فرق بين النقص في الفعل وبين القبح العقلي ) فيه ) فإن النقص في الأفعال هو القبح العقلي بعينه ) فيها ( وإنما تختلف العبارة) دون المعنى، فأصحابنا المنكرون للقبح العقلي كيف يتمسكون في دفع الكذب عن الكلام اللفظي بلزوم النقص في أفعاله تعالى
As for the impossibility of lying for Him (according to us, it is) by three aspects: The first: that it is deficiency (naqṣ), and deficiency upon God is impossible by consensus, and also it would follow, on the assumption that lying occurs in His speech, that we are more perfect than Him at some times, namely when we are truthful in our speech. This aspect only indicates that the internal speech (al-kalām al-nafsī) is an attribute subsisting in His essence which is truthful; otherwise a deficiency in His attribute would follow with the perfection of our attribute, and it does not indicate His truthfulness in the letters and words which He creates in a body indicating intended meanings. His saying: (And deficiency upon God is impossible by consensus). For if you say: there is no doubt that His not sending Noah, peace be upon him, to his people is possible because sending is not obligatory upon Him, rather it occurs by His choice, and denial is obstinacy, so the possibility of deficiency follows. I say: the possibility of deficiency only follows if it is possible to combine the non-sending with the utterance of His saying 'Indeed We sent Noah to his people' [71:1], which is prevented. And the impossibility of this combination does not negate the possibility of non-sending in itself, based on the principle of choice, so ponder. His saying: (He alluded to its refutation by his saying 'And know' etc.) It is not hidden that the apparent meaning of the author's saying 'And know' etc. is the objection to the first aspect mentioned in the text. And since it was directed against him that what necessarily follows from lying is deficiency in terms of His essential attribute, not deficiency in action, and the point of contention is the permissibility of the second, not the first, for it is impossible by consensus, as you will know. And you are aware that this is not free from contrivance. For deficiency in actions is exactly rational ugliness; only the expression differs, not the meaning. So how do our associates who deny rational ugliness argue in refuting lying from verbal speech by the necessity of deficiency in His actions?

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the first Ash'arite argument against divine lying, along with objections and responses. The first argument states that lying is a deficiency (naqṣ), and deficiency is impossible for God. Moreover, if lying occurred, humans would be more perfect when truthful, which is impossible. However, the author notes that this argument only proves the truthfulness of internal speech (kalām nafsī), not external speech (lafẓī). A commentary then raises an objection: consider God not sending Noah; that seems to imply possible deficiency. The commentator argues that the author's statement 'And know' addresses this: there is no difference between deficiency in action and rational ugliness; they are the same. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Deficiency in action and rational ugliness are two distinct concepts.
Correction: The author explicitly states they are the same; only the expression differs.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the relationship between deficiency in action (al-naqṣ fī al-fiʿl) and rational ugliness (al-qubḥ al-ʿaqlī)?
A) They are different concepts.
B) They are identical in meaning.
C) Deficiency is a subset of ugliness.
D) They are unrelated.

Key Takeaway
Equating deficiency in action with rational ugliness undermines the Ash'arite argument against verbal lying, revealing an internal inconsistency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you defend a theological position, do you ever rely on concepts you claim to reject? Where in your own beliefs might there be a similar inconsistency that you need to confront?
?
If deficiency in action and rational ugliness are the same, how does that change the way you view actions you consider 'ugly'? Are you willing to accept that your judgments of good and bad might not be as objective as you think?
?
What would it cost you to admit that an argument you've used to defend God's perfection actually undermines your own theological framework? Would you rather preserve the argument or be consistent?

The Ash'arite Second Argument: Eternality of Lying Precluding Truth
Presents the second Ash'arite argument: if lying were possible, it would be eternal, precluding truth, and the objection that this also precludes truth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second Ash'arite argument against the possibility of lying in God, the objection that it also proves truth impossible, and the response based on internal speech.

Core Idea
If God could lie, His lie would be eternal—and then truth could never exist.

This passage presents the second argument from the Ash'arite school defending the impossibility of lying in God. The argument uses the concept of eternality: any attribute of God must be eternal, and an eternal lie would make its opposite—truth—impossible. However, a powerful objection is raised: doesn't the same reasoning show that truth would also make lying impossible? The author addresses this by distinguishing between internal and external speech. Let's examine the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the logical flow: premise, consequence, objection, response.
The argument is condensed; take your time.
2
Identify the main argument
Find the claim that if lying were possible, it would be eternal, leading to the impossibility of truth. Why is that a problem?
The phrase 'ما ثبت قدمه امتنع عدمه' is key.
3
Examine the objection
Notice the objection that the same logic would prove truth impossible. How does that challenge the argument?
4
Understand the response
The response distinguishes between verbal and internal speech. Why does that matter? Reflect on the claim that we cannot judge contrary to our knowledge.
This is about kalām nafsī (internal speech).
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The commentary concludes that the proof shows internal speech must be truthful. What does this imply about God's attributes?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أنه لو اتصف بالكذب لكان كذبه قديماً إذ لا يقوم الحادث بذاته تعالى فيلزم أن يمتنع عليه الصدق ) المقابل لذلك الكذب، وإلا جاز زوال ذلك الكذب وهو محال (فإن ما ثبت قدمه امتنع عدمه اللازم) وهو امتناع الصدق عليه واللازم باطل فإنا نعلم بالضرورة أن من علم شيئاً أمكن) له ( أن يخبر عنه على ما هو عليه وهذا الوجه الثاني أيضاً (إنما يدل على كون الكلام النفسي صدقاً لأنه القديم، (وأما هذه العبارات) الدالة على الكلام قوله : ( واللازم باطل فإنا نعلم بالضرورة إلخ) فإن قلت هذا الدليل غير صحيح بجميع مقدماته إذ لو صح لدل على امتناع الصدق أيضا فإنه تعالى لو اتصف به لكان صدقه قديما فيلزم أن يمتنع عليه الكذب مع أنا نعلم أيضاً بالضرورة أن من علم شيئاً أمكن له أن يخبر عنه لا على عليه قلت : أجيب عنه بأن قوله : من علم شيئاً إلخ، ممنوع ودعوى الضرورة غير مسموعة، إذ ليس الكلام في الصدق والكذب اللفظيين حتى يمكنه ذلك بل في النفسانيين، ونحن نعلم بالوجدان أنا متى علمنا شيئاً فإنه يتعذر علينا أن نحكم بخلاف ما نعلمه، وهذا الجواب بعد ما قيل في بيان مغايرة مدلول الخبر للعلم من أن الرجل قد يخبر عما لا يعلمه بل يعلم خلافه محل بحث، ولو تم لدل من أول الأمر على امتناع قيام الكذب به ولا يحتاج إلى التشبث بلزوم امتناع الصدق بناء على أن ما ثبت قدمه امتنع عدمه، اللهم إلا أن يحمل الجواب على المنع والسند ويكون المنع بالنظر إلى خصوص الباري سبحانه، فتأمل. قوله : ( إنما يدل على كون الكلام النفسي صدقاً دلالته على صدقه باعتبار تعلقاته النوعية القديمة ظاهرة، وأما باعتبار تعلقاته الشخصية الحادثة إن أثبتها الأشعري فمحل بحث .
The second: that if He were described with lying, His lie would be eternal (qadīm), since the temporal does not subsist in His essence, so it would follow that truth (al-ṣidq) corresponding to that lying would be impossible for Him; otherwise the removal of that lying would be possible, which is impossible. For what is established as eternal, its non-existence is impossible. The necessary consequence is the impossibility of truth for Him, and the necessary consequence is false, for we know by necessity that whoever knows a thing can inform of it as it is. And this second aspect also only indicates that internal speech (al-kalām al-nafsī) is truthful because it is eternal. As for these expressions indicating speech... His saying: (And the necessary consequence is false, for we know by necessity etc.) For if you say: this proof is not correct in all its premises, because if it were sound, it would also prove the impossibility of truth, for if He were described with it, His truth would be eternal, so it would follow that lying is impossible for Him, while we also know by necessity that whoever knows a thing can inform of it not as it is? I say: it is answered that his saying 'whoever knows a thing etc.' is prevented (mamnūʿ), and the claim of necessity is not accepted, for the discussion is not about verbal truth and lying so that he could do that, but rather about the internal (nafsī). And we know intuitively that whenever we know a thing, it is impossible for us to judge contrary to our knowledge. This answer, after what was said in explaining the difference between the significance of the report and knowledge, that a man may report what he does not know, but rather knows its opposite, is a subject of discussion. And if it were sound, it would prove from the very beginning the impossibility of lying in Him and there would be no need for clinging to the necessity of the impossibility of truth, based on that what is established as eternal, its non-existence is impossible. Unless the answer is interpreted as prevention (manʿ) and the objection is considering the particularity of the Creator, so contemplate. His saying: (It only indicates that internal speech is truthful: its indication of its truthfulness in terms of its ancient generic relations is apparent; as for its individual temporal relations if al-Ashʿarī affirms them, they are a subject of discussion.)
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage presents the second Ash'arite argument: if lying could occur in God, His lie would be eternal because temporal attributes cannot subsist in Him. An eternal lie would then make truth impossible, which is false because we know that one who knows can inform truthfully. Thus, lying is impossible. However, an objection is raised: the same reasoning would prove truth impossible because if truth were eternal, it would preclude lying. The response distinguishes between verbal and internal speech: the argument applies to internal speech. We know intuitively that when we know something, we cannot judge contrary to our knowledge; thus, internal truth is necessary and internal lying is impossible. The commentator notes that this conclusion is sufficient without needing to argue from eternality. He also notes that the proof only shows internal speech is true based on its generic relations; individual temporal relations are debatable.

Key Terms
الكذب
الصدق
القديم
الكلام النفسي
الضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument proves that God cannot be truthful either.
Correction: The argument only aims to show that internal lying is impossible; internal truth is compatible with the argument.

Checkpoint
Why does the second Ash'arite argument claim that if lying were possible, truth would be impossible?
A) Because a lie would be a contingent attribute.
B) Because an eternal lie would preclude the existence of its opposite truth.
C) Because truth and lying cannot coexist in the same subject.
D) Because God cannot inform about what He knows.

Key Takeaway
The second Ash'arite argument shows that internal lying is impossible for God because an eternal lie would preclude truth, and internal knowledge prevents judging contrary to what one knows.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah could lie, His promises would be unreliable. How does the certainty that internal lying is impossible for Him change the way you read and trust His verses today?
?
The passage distinguishes between what you verbally say and what you internally know. Have you ever spoken a truth externally while internally doubting or hiding a different conviction? What does that reveal about your inner integrity before Allah?
?
The argument uses 'necessary knowledge' (al-ḍarūrah) that one who knows can inform truthfully. Do you live with that same necessary certainty about Allah's truthfulness, or do you allow doubts to creep in? What specific action will you take this week to strengthen that certainty?

The Second Argument: An Eternal Lie Is Impossible
Proof that lying cannot be attributed to God

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument for why lying cannot be attributed to God: because a lie would be eternal, and an eternal falsehood is impossible.

Core Idea
A lie in God would be eternal, but an eternal falsehood is impossible.

This lesson examines a powerful argument that eliminates the possibility of God lying. The argument builds on the Ash'ari principle that God's speech is an eternal attribute. If God could utter a false statement, that falsehood would also be eternal, yet an eternal falsehood is contradictory to truth. The passage also addresses a potential objection about the relation that grounds lying.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the full translation. Let the argument's flow come through before interpreting.
Notice how the commentary by al-Abhari is embedded.
2
Identify the key premise
The core premise is that contingent things cannot subsist in God's essence. This forces any attribute of God to be eternal.
3
Follow al-Abhari's elaboration
Al-Abhari explicitly states that if false speech were permissible, it would be eternal because of the impossibility of contingent attributes in God.
4
Understand the role of 'relation'
The relation (talluq) that makes speech informative is also eternal according to most Ash'aris, which blocks the objection that the lie might be contingent.
This is a key subtlety in the argument.
5
See how the argument refutes objections
The passage concludes that denying the eternity of the lie based on a contingent relation fails, so the argument stands.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( لكان) كذبه قديماً إذ لا يقوم الحادث بذاته (تعالى) المراد بالكذب الكلام الكاذب وبالصدق الكلام الصادق ولذا قال الأبهري في تقرير هذا الوجه الثاني : لو جاز عليه الكلام الكاذب لكان ذلك الكلام قديماً لامتناع قيام الحادث بذاته تعالى إلخ، ولا شك في كونه صفة حقيقية ثم التعلق الذي يتوقف عليه الاتصاف بالخبرية التي هي مناط الاتصاف بالكذب قديم عند جمهور الأشاعرة كما مر، وبهذا اندفع منع لزوم قدم الكذب مطلقاً بناء على توهم حدوث مبناه أعني التعلق ومنع كون الكذب صفة حقيقية قائمة به تعالى على ذلك التقدير حتى يلزم قيام الحوادث به تعالى فليتدبر
His statement: 'it would be' that his lie would be eternal, since the contingent cannot subsist by His essence. What is meant by lying is false speech and by truth is true speech. Therefore al-Abhari said in his explanation of this second aspect: if false speech were permissible for Him, that speech would be eternal because of the impossibility of the contingent subsisting by His essence, etc. There is no doubt that it is a real attribute. Then the relation upon which the description of being informative depends, which is the basis of the description of lying, is eternal according to the majority of Ash'aris, as has passed. By this is repelled the denial of the necessity of the eternity of lying absolutely, based on the illusion that its foundation (i.e., the relation) is contingent, and the denial that lying is a real attribute subsisting in Him on that estimation, so that the subsistence of contingents in Him would be necessary. So ponder.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument against the possibility of lying in God's speech. The commentator explains that if God were to utter a false statement, that statement would be eternal because it would be a real attribute of God, and contingent things cannot subsist in God's essence. Al-Abhari elaborates: if false speech were permissible for God, it would be eternal. Then the relation that makes the speech informative (which is the basis for calling it a lie) is also eternal according to most Ash'aris. This refutes any objection that the lie might be contingent due to a contingent relation. Thus, the argument that lying is impossible because it would entail an eternal false statement that would preclude truth is established. The commentator urges careful reflection.

Key Terms
الكذب قديم
قيام الحادث بذاته
التعلق
الخبرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the lie could be contingent if the relation (talluq) that grounds it is contingent.
Correction: The relation is also eternal according to the majority of Ash'aris, so the lie remains eternal. The argument relies on the impossibility of contingent attributes, not on the eternity of the lie itself.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The second argument shows that if God could lie, the lie would have to be eternal, but an eternal falsehood is impossible, so lying is not an attribute of God.

?
The argument shows that an eternal lie is impossible. But your lies are temporal—they pass. Yet, do you realize that each lie you utter is a contradiction of the truth that God's speech is perfect? How will you examine your speech today to ensure it aligns with the truth that God loves?

The Third Proof: Prophetic Testimony and the Circularity Objection
Al-Jurjani presents the third proof for God's truthfulness and answers the circularity objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof for God's truthfulness based on prophetic reports and how the miracle breaks the circularity objection.

Core Idea
Prophetic testimony proves God's truthfulness; the miracle confirms it without circularity.

In this passage, al-Jurjani presents the third proof for God's truthfulness, which relies on the prophetic report that God is truthful. He then addresses a key objection: if the prophet's truthfulness is known by God's confirmation, and God's truthfulness is needed to confirm the prophet, doesn't that create circularity? The answer lies in the miracle—a practical, non-verbal confirmation that bypasses the need for prior knowledge of God's truthfulness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation to get the flow of the argument.
Notice the structure: proof, objection, answer.
2
Identify the proof structure
The proof is based on the prophetic report known by necessity (tawatur). Find the claim that it requires no chain analysis.
3
Understand the circularity objection
The objection states that the prophet's truthfulness depends on God's confirmation, and God's truthfulness is needed to establish that, creating a loop.
This is a standard objection in kalam.
4
Grasp the answer via miracle
The answer distinguishes between verbal and practical confirmation. The miracle is a practical (fi'li) confirmation with customary indication, breaking the circularity.
The miracle directly indicates the prophet's truthfulness without relying on God's speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : وعليه الاعتماد) لصحته ودلالته على الصدق في الكلام النفسي واللفظي معا) خبر النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام). بكونه صادقا في كلامه كله (وذلك) أي خبره عليه السلام بصدقه (مما يعلم بالضرورة من الدين فلا حاجة إلى بيان إسناده) وصحته ولا إلى تعيين ذلك الخبر بل نقول: تواتر عن الأنبياء عليهم السلام كونه تعالى صادقا كما تواتر عنهم كونه تعالى متكلما (فإن قيل: صدق النبي إنما يعلم بتصديقه تعالى إياه و (إنما يدل تصديقه) إياه، (على الصدق) أي صدق النبي (إذا امتنع عليه) تعالى (الكذب) ووجب يكون كلامه صدقا، فصدق النبي إنما يعرف بصدق الله تعالى (فيلزم الدور) إذا ثبت صدقه تعالى بصدق النبي كما فعلتم (قلنا: التصديق بالمعجزة) تصديق فعلي لا قولي ودلالتها على التصديق دلالة عادية لا يتطرق إليها شبهة كما ستقف عليه،
The third: And upon it is reliance) for its soundness and its indication of truthfulness in both internal speech (al-kalam al-nafsi) and verbal speech (al-kalam al-lafzi) together) the report of the Prophet, peace be upon him). That he is truthful in all his speech (and that) i.e., his report, peace be upon him, regarding his truthfulness (is known by necessity from religion so there is no need to explain its chain) and its authenticity, nor to specify that report. Rather we say: It is mutawatir from the prophets, peace be upon them, that He, Exalted, is truthful, just as it is mutawatir from them that He, Exalted, is a speaker. (If it is said: The truthfulness of the prophet is only known by God's confirming him, and His confirmation indicates truthfulness (i.e., the prophet's truthfulness) only if it is impossible for Him, Exalted, to lie) and His speech must be truthful. So the truthfulness of the prophet is only known by God's truthfulness. (Then circularity follows) if His truthfulness is established by the prophet's truthfulness as you did. (We say: Confirmation by the miracle) is a practical confirmation, not a verbal one, and its indication of confirmation is a customary indication that no doubt reaches, as you will come to know.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof for God's truthfulness, centered on prophetic testimony, followed by an objection regarding circularity and its answer. The author states that reliance is placed on the prophet's report because it is sound and indicates truthfulness in both internal and external speech. The prophet's truthfulness is known by necessity from religion through tawatur, requiring no chain analysis. An objection argues that if the prophet's truthfulness depends on God's confirmation, and God's confirmation implies His truthfulness only if lying is impossible for Him, then proving God's truthfulness by the prophet's truthfulness would be circular. The answer is that the confirmation is by miracles (mu'jiza), which is a practical (fi'li) confirmation, not verbal (qawli). Its indication is customary ('adi), not logical, so no circularity arises. The miracle directly compels acknowledgment of the prophet's truthfulness without relying on prior knowledge of God's truthfulness.

Key Terms
الثالث
خبر النبي
ضرورة الدين
تواتر
دور
تصديق بالمعجزة
تصديق فعلي
دلالة عادية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof from prophetic testimony is circular because it presupposes God's truthfulness to prove the prophet's truthfulness.
Correction: The answer shows that the miracle is a practical confirmation, not a verbal one, so it does not require prior knowledge of God's truthfulness; it directly testifies to the prophet's truthfulness through customary indication.

Checkpoint
How does the answer avoid the charge of circularity in proving God's truthfulness?
A) By relying on the necessity of religion
B) By using a practical confirmation (miracle) that is not verbal
C) By appealing to tawatur
D) By admitting the prophet's truthfulness is self-evident

Key Takeaway
The third proof for God's truthfulness relies on the mutawatir prophetic report, and the miracle provides a practical confirmation that avoids circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Have you ever felt that your faith in Allah's truthfulness is based on a circular reasoning—believing because the Prophet said so, and believing the Prophet because he is from Allah? How does the concept of miracle as a practical confirmation from Allah break that circle in your heart, not just in theory?

The prophet's truthfulness is known by necessity from religion through tawatur. Do you personally feel that certainty in your soul, or do you sometimes waver? What practical step can you take today to strengthen that certainty, based on the 'customary indication' of the miracles you witness in your life or hear about?

When you read about the miracles of the Prophet (e.g., the Quran, splitting of the moon), do you allow them to transform your reliance on his reports, or do you treat them as just historical facts?

Ash'ari's Divine Speech: Misunderstanding and Resolution
Al-Jurjani explains the ashab's misunderstanding and resolves it by an inclusive interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the ashab's interpretation of al-Ash'ari's definition of divine speech leads to corrupt consequences and how al-Ash'ari's inclusive internal speech resolves these issues.

Core Idea
Divine speech includes both meaning and words, avoiding corrupt consequences.

In this passage, al-Jurjani addresses a serious misunderstanding of al-Ash'ari's view on divine speech. Some scholars thought al-Ash'ari separated the Quranic text from God's eternal speech, but this leads to unacceptable theological consequences. Al-Jurjani shows that the correct reading affirms that God's internal speech encompasses both meaning and words, preserving the Quran's status as God's real speech.

Guided Path
1
Understand the ashab's misinterpretation
Read the passage to see how the ashab understood al-Ash'ari's definition: they held that words are only metaphorically speech, temporal, and not God's real speech.
This is the problem that leads to corrupt consequences.
2
Identify the corrupt consequences
The passage lists three specific consequences: not declaring the denier of the Quran as a disbeliever, absence of opposition and challenge with God's real speech, and the recited Quran not being His speech in reality.
These consequences are considered 'corrupt' because they contradict clear religious knowledge.
3
Grasp the necessity of reinterpretation
Al-Jurjani argues that to avoid these consequences, we must reinterpret al-Ash'ari's words, understanding that he intended the second meaning of 'meaning' (ma'na).
This reinterpretation is not arbitrary but driven by the need to preserve the Quran's status.
4
Learn the inclusive definition
The correct view is that internal speech (kalam nafsi) is inclusive of both word and meaning, subsisting in God's essence, and expressed in writing, recitation, and memorization.
This definition resolves all the problems mentioned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنما تسمى كلاماً مجازاً لدلالتها على ما هو كلام حقيقي حتى صرحوا بأن الألفاظ حادثة على مذهبه أيضاً لكنها ليست كلامه حقيقة وهذا الذي فهموه من كلام الشيخ له لوازم كثيرة فاسدة كعدم إكفار من أنكر كلامية ما بين دفتي المصحف مع أنه علم من الدين ضرورة كونه كلام الله تعالى حقيقة وكعدم المعارضة والتحدي بكلام الله تعالى الحقيقي، وكعدم كون المقروء والمحفوظ كلامه حقيقة إلى غير ذلك مما لا يخفى على المتفطن في الأحكام الدينية فوجب حمل كلام الشيخ على أنه أراد به المعنى الثاني، فيكون الكلام النفسي عنده أمراً شاملاً للفظ والمعنى جميعاً قائماً بذات الله تعالى وهو مكتوب في المصاحف مقروء بالألسن محفوظ في الصدور، وهو غير الكتابة والقراءة والحفظ الحادثة
They are only called speech metaphorically because they indicate what is real speech, until they declared that the words are temporal according to his school as well but they are not His speech in reality. And this understanding they derived from the Shaykh's words has many corrupt consequences, such as not declaring the denier of the speech of what is between the two covers of the Quran to be a disbeliever, even though it is known from religion by necessity that it is God's speech in reality, and such as the absence of opposition and challenge with God's real speech, and such as that what is recited and memorized is not His speech in reality, and other things that are not hidden from the attentive in religious rulings. So it is necessary to interpret the Shaykh's words as that he intended the second meaning, so that internal speech according to him is something inclusive of both word and meaning together, subsisting in God's essence, and it is written in the copies, recited on tongues, preserved in breasts, and it is other than the writing, recitation, and memorization which are temporal.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage explains the ashab's misunderstanding of al-Ash'ari's definition of divine speech and its resolution. The ashab held that expressions (alfaz) are called speech only metaphorically (majazan) because they indicate the real speech, and they declared that the words are temporal but not God's speech in reality. This interpretation leads to several corrupt consequences (lawazim fasida): (1) not declaring takfir on someone who denies that what is between the two covers of the Quran is God's speech, even though it is known from religion by necessity that it is God's speech; (2) the absence of opposition and challenge (mu'arada and tahaddi) with God's real speech; (3) that what is recited and memorized is not His speech in reality. To avoid these, al-Ash'ari must be understood as intending the second meaning of 'meaning' (ma'na), i.e., that internal speech (kalam nafsi) is inclusive of both word and meaning, subsisting in God's essence, and it is written in copies, recited on tongues, and preserved in breasts, yet distinct from the temporal writing, recitation, and memorization.

Key Terms
مجاز - metaphor
الألفاظ - words/expressions
لوازم فاسدة - corrupt consequences
إكفار - declaring disbelief
المعارضة والتحدي - opposition and challenge
الكلام النفسي الشامل - inclusive internal speech

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Ash'ari's definition of divine speech separates the Quranic text from God's eternal speech, making the text merely temporal and metaphorical.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari's intended definition includes both meaning and words in internal speech, so the Quranic text is God's real speech, written, recited, and preserved.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the ashab's interpretation of al-Ash'ari's definition of speech is rejected?
A) It contradicts tawatur
B) It leads to corrupt consequences such as not declaring deniers of the Quran as disbelievers
C) It makes the Quran temporal
D) It denies the existence of internal speech

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari's internal speech includes both meaning and words, making the Quran written and recited His real speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Are you living with the certainty that every letter you utter is His speech? Commit today to reciting with that conviction, as if hearing it from Him directly.

The ashab's misinterpretation led to severe consequences like refusing to declare deniers of the Quran as disbelievers. Pray for clarity and courage to abandon such compromises.

Defending Inclusive Divine Speech: Commonality of Type and Individual
Al-Jurjani presents an objection to the inclusive definition of kalam and answers it by positing commonality between type and individual.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani defends the inclusive definition of divine speech by responding to an objection through positing commonality (ishtirak) between the type and the individual instance.

Core Idea
The term 'kalam' is common between the eternal type and temporal instances, not metaphorical.

This lesson presents a precise objection-answer unit from al-Jurjani's commentary. The objection tries to show that defining divine speech as including both word and meaning leads to contradictions. The answer cleverly posits that the term 'kalam' is shared (mushtarak) between the eternal attribute (type) and the created instances (individuals), preserving both realities. By working through this argument, you will see how kalam reasoning defends nuanced theological positions.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its structure
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Notice that the objection presents three possible implications of the inclusive definition, each leading to an unacceptable consequence.
The objection is a trilemma: three possibilities, all false.
2
Understand each possibility
Identify each of the three possibilities: (1) if 'kalam' names the specific individual in God, then recited speech is not His; (2) if it names the type, then application to the specific individual is metaphorical; (3) if it is general positing with particular subject, then divine speech would be temporal.
Each possibility is refuted by the objector as false.
3
Examine the answer: commonality
The answer posits ishtirak (commonality) between the type and the specific individual. That means the term 'kalam' is used equally for both, not metaphorically, and without temporal implications.
This avoids the contradictions and preserves the reality of both eternal and temporal speech.
4
Reflect on the resolution
Consider why this answer works: by sharing the term, we can refer to both God's eternal speech and the created Quran without equivocation or metaphor. This maintains the inclusive definition.
This is a classic kalam solution using the notion of commonality (ishtirak).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فيكون الكلام النفسي عنده أمراً شاملاً لللفظ والمعنى اعترض عليه بأن كلام الله تعالى إن كان اسماً لذلك الشخص القائم بذاته تعالى لزم أن لا يكون ما قرأناه كلامه تعالى بل مثله ، واللازم باطل القطع بأن ما يقرأه كل واحد منا وهو الكلام المنزل على النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام بلسان جبرائيل عليه السلام وإن كان اسماً لنوع القائم يلزم أن يكون إطلاقه على ذلك الشخص بخصوصه مجازاً فيجوز نفيه عنه حقيقة، وإن جعل من قبيل كون الموضوع له خاصاً والوضع عاملاً يلزم أن يوصف كلامه تعالى بالحدوث حقيقة أيضاً، وأجيب بالتزام اشتراكه بين النوع وذلك الفرد الخاص .
His statement: (So the internal speech according to him is something inclusive of word and meaning. It was objected that if God's speech is the name for that specific individual subsisting in His essence, then it would follow that what we recite is not His speech but a likeness of it, and the consequent is false. The definitive statement that what each of us recites, which is the speech revealed to the prophet, peace be upon him, by the tongue of Gabriel, peace be upon him, if it is the name for the type of the subsisting, then it would follow that its application to that specific individual is metaphorical, so its denial of it in reality would be permitted. And if it is made from the category of the subject of predication being particular and the positing being general, then it would follow that His speech would be described as temporal in reality as well. And it was answered by positing the commonality between the type and that specific individual.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is an objection-answer unit regarding the inclusive definition of kalam nafsi. The objection considers three possibilities: (1) if 'God's speech' is a name for the specific individual (shakhs) subsisting in God, then what we recite would be a likeness, not His speech—false; (2) if it is a name for the type (naw') of that subsisting, then applying it to the specific individual would be metaphorical (majaz), allowing denial in reality—false; (3) if made from the category where the subject (mawdu' lahu) is particular and the positing (wad') is general (perhaps meaning that the term is assigned to a particular meaning but used generically?), then His speech would be described as temporal in reality—also false. The answer is to posit commonality (ishtirak) between the type and that specific individual. That is, the term 'kalam' is used for both the eternal attribute and the temporal instances without metaphor, thereby preserving the reality of both.

Key Terms
الشخص القائم - the specific subsisting individual
النوع - the type
المجاز - metaphor
الوضع العام - general positing
الاشتراك - commonality

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the inclusive definition is self-contradictory because it labels both eternal and temporal realities as 'kalam'.
Correction: The answer resolves this by positing that the term 'kalam' is common (mushtarak) between the type (eternal) and the individual (temporal), so it is not contradictory but rather a linguistically valid polysemy.

Checkpoint
What is the answer to the objection that if God's speech is a specific individual, then what we recite is not His speech?
A) That the term is metaphorical
B) That the term is common between the type and the individual
C) That the speech is temporal
D) That the speech is only the meaning

Key Takeaway
The inclusive definition of divine speech is defended by positing that the term 'kalam' is common between the eternal type and temporal instances, avoiding metaphor or contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you ever pause to consider that the very words you utter are, in a real sense, God's eternal speech? How does the concept of commonality between type and individual help you internalize that reality today?
?

?
Are there areas of your faith where you have settled for a 'metaphorical' connection to God—treating His attributes as symbols rather than realities? What would it cost you to accept the commonality that the scholars defend here, and what would it give you?

The Written and Recited Quran: Temporal Expression of the Eternal Word
Al-Jurjani defines and relates writing and reading to the eternal word.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quran can be both written/recited as a temporal creation and yet be the eternal word of Allah.

Core Idea
The same Quranic word is both temporal in recitation and eternal as God's attribute.

This passage from al-Jurjani's commentary tackles a subtle point: how can the Quran, which we write, recite, and memorize, also be the eternal speech of Allah? The key is in carefully defining terms like 'writing,' 'reading,' and 'word.' Al-Jurjani explains that the temporal word (the recited sounds) and the eternal word (God's attribute) are united in meaning while distinct in form.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Al-Jurjani is explaining the phrase 'written in copies, recited on tongues' from the creed. This phrase points to the Quran being both a physical book and an eternal attribute.
2
Define key terms
Identify the definitions of kitaba (writing), nuqush (marks), lafz (word), qira'a (reading), and ma'na (meaning). Notice how each term is used in the argument.
Pay attention to the distinction between the marks and the word itself.
3
Trace the argument on reading
Al-Jurjani gives two senses of reading: either you mention a thing by its word (then the recited object is the meaning) or you mention the word itself (then the recited object is the word). This allows the recitation to refer to the eternal meaning.
4
Understand the conclusion
The temporal recited word applies generally to both the temporal and the eternal because the word is considered one in all locations. The distinction is only in the letters and forms, not in the word itself.
This preserves the unity of the Quran while accounting for its temporal manifestation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (مكتوب) في المصاحف مقروء بالألسن) الكتابة تصوير اللفظ بالنقوش المخصوصة فالثابت في المصحف هو النقوش والمكتوب هو اللفظ والقراءة إن كان ذكر الشيء بلفظه فالمقروء هو المعنى وإن كان ذكر اللفظ فهو اللفظ ثم اللفظ المقروء والمحفوظ الحادث، يعم والقديم بناء على أن اللفظ يعد واحداً في المحال كلها ولا يعتبر متبايناً إلا بتباين الحروف والهيآت .
His statement: (Written in the copies, recited on tongues). Writing is the depiction of the word (lafz) with specific marks. So what is established in the copy is the marks, and what is written is the word. Reading (qira'a): if it is mentioning the thing by its word, then what is recited is the meaning; if it is mentioning the word, then it is the word. Then the recited and memorized word, which is temporal, applies generally and the eternal, based on the fact that the word is counted as one in all locations and is not considered distinct except by the distinction of letters and forms.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage defines the terms 'maktub' (written) and 'maqru' (recited) as applied to the Quran. Writing (kitaba) is the depiction of the word (lafz) with specific marks (nuqush). What is established in the copy (mushaf) is the marks, but the written object is the word. Reading (qira'a) has two senses: if one is mentioning the thing by its word (dhikr al-shay' bi-lafzihi), then the recited object is the meaning (ma'na); if one is mentioning the word itself (dhikr al-lafz), then the recited object is the word (lafz). Then it states that the recited and memorized word, which is temporal (hadith), applies generally (ya'umm) to both the temporal and the eternal, because the word (lafz) is considered one (wahid) in all locations (mahall) and is not considered distinct (mutabayin) except by the distinction of letters and forms. This allows the same word to be both temporal (in recitation) and eternal (as God's attribute).

Key Terms
الكتابة
النقوش
اللفظ
القراءة
المقروء
المعنى
المحفوظ
المحال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The written or recited Quran is purely temporal and has no connection to the eternal attribute of speech.
Correction: The passage explains that the temporal recited word applies to both the temporal and eternal aspects because the word is considered one across locations; it is only the letters and forms that are distinct.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is considered established in the copy (mushaf)?
A) The word (lafz)
B) The meaning (ma'na)
C) The marks (nuqush)
D) The eternal speech

Key Takeaway
The written and recited Quran is temporal in form but refers to the eternal word of Allah, united in the concept of the word itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you consciously connect the temporal sounds you produce with the eternal word of Allah, or do you treat the recitation as just another worldly act? How can you deepen that connection starting today?
?
If the word of Allah is one across all recitations, what does that imply about the unity of the global Muslim community reciting the same Quran? How does this truth challenge you to see every recitation—yours and others'—as part of a single divine address?
?
When you hear the Quran recited in a language you do not understand, do you still perceive it as the eternal word, or does your heart become disconnected? What would it take for you to listen with the awareness that the eternal meaning is present regardless of the physical sound?

Distinguishing the Eternal Word from Temporal Utterance
Al-Jurjani refutes the objection that letter ordering implies temporality by distinguishing the word from the utterance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Jurjani defends the pre-eternity of the divine word by distinguishing between the eternal word itself and the temporal utterance.

Core Idea
The ordering of letters applies only to the temporal utterance, not to the eternal word.

One of the strongest objections against the doctrine that the Quran is uncreated is that its letters are ordered, and ordering implies temporality. Al-Jurjani provides a precise answer: the ordering belongs only to the utterance (talfuz), which is our recitation, not to the eternal word (lafz) subsisting in God. This distinction preserves the divine attribute from temporal attributes while acknowledging the temporal nature of our recitation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Start by reading the objection in the passage: that letter ordering is only in the utterance, not in the spoken word, and that this distinction is irrational.
Notice the objection compares it to imagining a movement with simultaneous parts – a clear straw man.
2
Identify the answer
The answer begins with 'yandafi'' (it is repelled). Read carefully what Al-Jurjani says about the terms 'lafz' and 'talfuz'.
The key is that 'lafz' is ambiguous; it must be specified.
3
Understand the two meanings
Recognize that 'lafz' can mean either the word subsisting in God (eternal) or the utterance subsisting in us (temporal). The answer uses 'talfuz' to clearly indicate the temporal one.
This linguistic precision is typical in kalam.
4
Link to the analogy
Reflect on the analogy of a source expression (nisba masdariyya) which is non-fixed. This helps explain why the temporal utterance is non-fixed and thus can have ordering.
The text cuts off but the main argument is complete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فجوابه أن ذلك الترتب إلخ ) وقد يقال: القول بأن ترتب الحروف إنما هو في التلفظ دون الملفوظ فالتلفظ حادث دون الملفوظ أمر خارج عن طور العقل وما ذلك إلا مثل أن يتصوّر حركة يكون أجزاؤها مجتمعة في الوجود لا يكون لبعضها تقدم على بعض، ويندفع بما قيل من أن المراد باللفظ اللفظ القائم به تعالى والتلفظ اللفظ القائم بنا عبر عنه بالتلفظ فرقا بينهما، وإشعاراً بأن اللفظ الحادث كالنسبة المصدرية لكونه غير قار ولولا هذا الاعتبار لكان القول بقدم
His statement: (So the answer is that that ordering etc.) And it may be said: The statement that the ordering of letters is only in the utterance (talfuz) and not in the spoken word (malafuz), so the utterance is temporal while the spoken word is not, is something outside the scope of reason. That is nothing but like imagining a movement whose parts are simultaneous in existence, with no precedence of some over others. And it is repelled by what is said: that by 'word' (lafz) is meant the word subsisting in Him, Exalted, and by 'utterance' (talfuz) is meant the word subsisting in us, and it is called 'talfuz' to differentiate between them, and to indicate that the temporal word is like the source expression (al-nisba al-masdariyya) because it is non-fixed. And were it not for this consideration, the statement of pre-eternity... [incomplete]
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and answer regarding the ordering of letters. The objection is that claiming the ordering of letters is only in the utterance (talfuz) and not in the spoken word (malafuz) is irrational—it likens it to imagining a movement with simultaneous parts. The answer (yandafi') is that by 'word' (lafz) is meant the word subsisting in God (al-lafz al-qa'im bihi ta'ala), and by 'utterance' (talfuz) is meant the word subsisting in us (al-lafz al-qa'im bina). The term 'talfuz' is used to differentiate between the two and to indicate that the temporal word is like a source expression (al-nisba al-masdariyya) because it is non-fixed (ghayr qar). The text cuts off before completing the thought, but the main point is clear: the ordering does not apply to the eternal word, only to the temporal utterance, which is distinct from the divine attribute.

Key Terms
الترتب - ordering
الحروف - letters
التلفظ - utterance
الملفوظ - spoken word
اللفظ القائم به - word subsisting in Him
النسبة المصدرية - source expression

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the ordering of letters in the Quran proves it is temporal, because ordering implies creation.
Correction: The ordering applies only to the temporal utterance (our recitation), not to the eternal word subsisting in God. The eternal word has no temporal ordering.

Checkpoint
What is the key distinction made in answering the objection about letter ordering?
A) Between word (lafz) and utterance (talfuz)
B) Between written and recited
C) Between eternal and temporal meaning
D) Between divine and human

Key Takeaway
The eternal divine word has no temporal ordering; ordering belongs only to the temporal human utterance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does this distinction shape your concentration and humility during recitation? Commit now to a single verse today and, as you say it, consciously remember that its essence is eternal even as your voice is temporal.
?
The passage admits the objection that this distinction seems irrational—like imagining a movement with simultaneous parts. Have you ever been tempted to dismiss theological distinctions as 'just semantics' rather than wrestling with them? What would it cost you to take this particular distinction seriously, especially in your understanding of the Quran's divine origin? Pledge to read the next surah you recite with the awareness that its letters are ordered in your utterance but un-ordered in God's eternal attribute.
?
If the ordering of letters is not in the eternal word itself, then the presence of a particular ayah in your mushaf is a temporal manifestation of something timeless. Does that change how you view the physical pages? How should it affect your treatment of the mushaf as more than paper and ink? Reflect on this before your next prayer and let it lead you to greater reverence.

The Nature of Divine Speech and the Ruling on Denying the Mushaf
Objection and response regarding the nature of divine speech and takfir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct interpretation of divine speech (kalam nafsi) and why denying that the Mushaf is God's speech is not necessarily disbelief according to most Ash'aris.

Core Idea
Internal speech includes both meaning and wording; denying the Mushaf is not kufr if one believes it is created and points to God's attribute.

This passage addresses a critical objection: if divine speech is only internal (nafsi), does that mean the Quran in our hands is not truly God's speech? The author explains the corrupt implications of such a view and then clarifies that denying the Mushaf is only kufr if one denies the attribute itself. Most Ash'aris hold that the words are created yet still indicate God's real speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the objection and response.
Pay attention to the terms kalam nafsi and lafz.
2
Identify the corrupt implications
The passage lists three corrupt implications of a literal interpretation: not declaring takfir on deniers, absence of challenge, and that recited/memorized is not His speech. Note each.
3
Understand the solution: the second meaning
The Shaykh's words are interpreted to mean that internal speech encompasses both meaning and wording as one attribute, while the written/recited forms are created mediums.
The order of letters is only in utterance, not in the attribute.
4
Clarify the ruling on takfir
The commentary distinguishes belief that the Mushaf is not God's speech outright (kufr) from belief that it is created and indicates His attribute (not kufr).
This is the position of most Ash'aris.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنما تسمى كلاماً مجازاً لدلالتها على ما هو كلام حقيقي حتى صرحوا بأن الألفاظ حادثة على مذهبه أيضاً لكنها ليست كلامه حقيقة وهذا الذي فهموه من كلام الشيخ له لوازم كثيرة فاسدة كعدم إكفار من أنكر كلامية ما بين دفتي المصحف مع أنه علم من الدين ضرورة كونه كلام الله تعالى حقيقة وكعدم المعارضة والتحدي بكلام الله تعالى الحقيقي، وكعدم كون المقروء والمحفوظ كلامه حقيقة إلى غير ذلك مما لا يخفى على المتفطن في الأحكام الدينية فوجب حمل كلام الشيخ على أنه أراد به المعنى الثاني، فيكون الكلام النفسي عنده أمراً شاملاً للفظ والمعنى جميعاً قائماً بذات الله تعالى وهو مكتوب في المصاحف مقروء بالألسن محفوظ في الصدور، وهو غير الكتابة والقراءة والحفظ الحادثة وما يقال من أن الحروف والألفاظ مترتبة متعاقبة فجوابه أن ذلك الترتب إنما هو في التلفظ مساعدة الآلة، فالتلفظ بسبب عدم
قوله : ( كعدم إكفار من أنكر كلامية ما بين دفتي المصحف واعلم أن إكفار منكرها إنما هو إذا اعتقد أنه ليس كلام الله تعالى بمعنى أنه ليس صفة قائمة بل هو دال على ما هو صفة حقيقية قائمة به جل وعلا، وهو من مبدعات الله تعالى ومخترعاته بأن أوجده في لسان الملك، أو لسان النبي عليهما السلام وأوجد نقوشاً دالة عليه في اللوح المحفوظ، فليس من الكفر في شيء بل هو مذهب أكثر الأشاعرة فلا أن ينبغي يتوهم كونه كفرا .
They are only called speech metaphorically because they indicate what is true speech, to the extent that they declared that words are created according to his school as well, but they are not His speech in reality. And this which they understood from the statement of the Shaykh has many corrupt implications, such as: not declaring as disbeliever one who denies that what is between the two covers of the Mushaf is speech, even though it is known from the religion by necessity that it is the speech of Allah in reality; and such as the absence of opposition and challenge with the true speech of Allah; and such as that what is recited and memorized is not His speech in reality; and other things that are not hidden from the discerning in religious rulings. So it is necessary to interpret the statement of the Shaykh as meaning that he intended the second meaning, such that internal speech (al-kalam al-nafsi) according to him is something encompassing both the wording (lafz) and the meaning (ma'na) together, subsisting in the essence of Allah, and it is written in the Mushafs, recited by tongues, memorized in hearts, and it is distinct from the created writing, reading, and memorization. And what is said about the letters and words being ordered and successive, its answer is that that order is only in the utterance (al-talfuz) with the assistance of the instrument, so the utterance is due to the absence... Commentary: (Regarding his statement: 'such as not declaring as disbeliever...') Know that declaring as a disbeliever the one who denies it is only if he believes that it is not the speech of Allah in the sense that it is not an attribute subsisting [in Him], but rather it indicates what is a real attribute subsisting in Him, and it is from among Allah's creations and inventions, by Him bringing it into existence in the tongue of the angel or the tongue of the prophet, peace be upon them, and He brought into existence shapes indicating it in the Preserved Tablet. So it is not disbelief at all, rather it is the position of most Ash'aris. So one should not suppose it to be disbelief.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage first presents the objection that interpreting divine speech as only internal (nafsi) without the external words leads to several corrupt consequences: it would mean not declaring as disbeliever those who deny the Mushaf is God's speech, would negate the concept of challenge (tahaddi) with the true speech, and would imply that what is recited and memorized is not truly His speech. To avoid these, the author argues that the Shaykh's words must be interpreted as meaning that internal speech (al-kalam al-nafsi) includes both the meaning and the wording, existing in God's essence, while being written, recited, and memorized in a created manner. Then, in the commentary (qawluh), it is clarified that denying the Mushaf as God's speech only constitutes disbelief if one denies that it is a real attribute subsisting in God. If one holds that it is created by God and placed in the angel's tongue or the Prophet's tongue, then it is not disbelief but the view of most Ash'aris. Thus, the passage guards against misinterpreting the nature of divine speech and the ruling on those who adopt a mistaken view of its creation.

لفظ
معنى
إكفار
مصحف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying that the Mushaf is God's speech is always kufr.
Correction: It is only kufr if one denies that the Quran is God's eternal attribute. If one believes the words are created and indicate God's speech, it is not kufr.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Denying the Mushaf is only kufr if one denies that the Quran is God's eternal attribute; most Ash'aris hold that the words are created yet still indicate God's real speech.

Internal Speech: Universal or Particular?
Objection and answer regarding internal speech as particular or universal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection against internal speech being a particular attribute and the Ash'ari answer that the term is equivocal between the universal and the particular.

Core Idea
God's speech is neither a mere universal nor a particular—it is both.

Sanusi begins by considering an objection: if God's speech is a particular attribute subsisting in Him, then what we recite cannot be His speech but only a likeness. Conversely, if it is a universal, applying it to a specific instance becomes metaphorical. The author defends the view that 'speech' is equivocal between the universal and the particular, thereby preserving both the eternity of God's attribute and the reality of our recited words.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the three possibilities: (1) speech is a particular attribute, (2) it is a universal, (3) it is a hybrid where the named is particular but coinage is general. Each leads to a problem.
The objection is targeting the definition of internal speech as including wording and meaning.
2
Identify the answer given
The answer is that the term is equivocal (ishtirak) between the universal and the particular. This means the same word can refer to both without contradiction.
This is a common Ash'ari solution for attributes.
3
Reflect on the implications
Why does this answer preserve both the eternity of God's attribute and the reality of our recited words? The equivocation allows the term to apply to both the eternal essence and the created instance without category error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فيكون الكلام النفسي عنده أمراً شاملاً للفظ والمعنى اعترض عليه بأن كلام الله تعالى إن كان اسماً لذلك الشخص القائم بذاته تعالى لزم أن لا يكون ما قرأناه كلامه تعالى بل مثله ، واللازم باطل القطع بأن ما يقرأه كل واحد منا وهو الكلام المنزل على النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام بلسان جبرائيل عليه السلام وإن كان اسماً لنوع القائم يلزم أن يكون إطلاقه على ذلك الشخص بخصوصه مجازاً فيجوز نفيه عنه حقيقة، وإن جعل من قبيل كون الموضوع له خاصاً والوضع عاملاً يلزم أن يوصف كلامه تعالى بالحدوث حقيقة أيضاً، وأجيب بالتزام اشتراكه بين النوع وذلك الفرد الخاص .
قوله : (مكتوب) في المصاحف مقروء بالألسن) الكتابة تصوير اللفظ بالنقوش المخصوصة فالثابت في المصحف هو النقوش والمكتوب هو اللفظ والقراءة إن كان ذكر الشيء بلفظه فالمقروء هو المعنى وإن كان ذكر اللفظ فهو اللفظ ثم اللفظ المقروء والمحفوظ الحادث، يعم والقديم بناء على أن اللفظ يعد واحداً في المحال كلها ولا يعتبر متبايناً إلا بتباين الحروف والهيآت .
قوله : ( فجوابه أن ذلك الترتب إلخ ) وقد يقال: القول بأن ترتب الحروف إنما هو في التلفظ دون الملفوظ فالتلفظ حادث دون الملفوظ أمر خارج عن طور العقل وما ذلك إلا مثل أن يتصوّر حركة يكون أجزاؤها مجتمعة في الوجود لا يكون لبعضها تقدم على بعض، ويندفع بما قيل من أن المراد باللفظ اللفظ القائم به تعالى والتلفظ اللفظ القائم بنا عبر عنه بالتلفظ فرقا بينهما
،
وإشعاراً بأن اللفظ الحادث كالنسبة المصدرية لكونه غير قار ولولا هذا الاعتبار لكان القول بقدم

حادث ولا دلة الدلة على الحدوث يجب حملها على حدوثه دون حدوث الملفوظ الأدلة، وهذا الذي ذكرناه وإن كان مخالفا لما عليه متأخرو أصحابنا لا أنه بعد التأمل تعرف حقيقته ثم كلامه وهذا المحمل لكلام الشيخ مما اختاره الشيخ محمد الشهرستاني في كتابه المسمى بنهاية الإقدام ولا شبهة في أنه أقرب إلى الأحكام الظاهرية المنسوبة إلى قواعد الملة .
Commentary: (Regarding his statement: 'so internal speech according to him is something encompassing the wording and the meaning') It was objected against him: If the speech of Allah is a name for that particular [attribute] subsisting in His essence, then it would follow that what we read is not His speech but rather its likeness, and the consequent is false definitely; but if it is a name for a universal of that [attribute], then it would follow that its application to that particular person is metaphorical, so its denial of it would be permissible in reality. And if it is made to be of the type where the thing named is particular and the coinage is general, then it would follow that His speech would be described as temporal in reality as well. And he answered by committing to its being equivocal between the universal and that particular individual. Commentary: (Regarding his statement: 'written in the Mushafs, recited by tongues') Writing is the depiction of the wording with specific shapes. So what is established in the Mushaf is the shapes, and what is written is the wording. As for reading, if it is the mention of the thing by its wording, then what is read is the meaning; and if it is the mention of the wording, then it is the wording. Then the recited and memorized wording is temporal, covering both the universal and the eternal based on the fact that the wording is considered one in all instances and is not considered distinct except by the distinctness of the letters and forms. Commentary: (Regarding his statement: 'so its answer is that this order etc.') And it may be said: The statement that the order of the letters is only in the utterance (talfuz) without the uttered word (malfulz), so the utterance is temporal without the uttered word, is something beyond the scope of reason. That is only like imagining a motion whose parts are simultaneous in existence, none of them being prior to another. And it is repelled by what was said: what is meant by 'the word' is the word subsisting in Him, and 'utterance' is the word subsisting in us; he expressed it as 'utterance' to differentiate between them and to indicate that the temporal word is like an infinitive (al-nisba al-masdariyya) because it is non-permanent. Were it not for this consideration, the statement of the temporal... and the evidence indicating temporality must be interpreted as indicating its temporality, not the temporality of the uttered word. And what we have mentioned, although contrary to the later [view] of our companions, after reflection you will know its truth and then his statement. And this interpretation of the Shaykh's words is what Shaykh Muhammad al-Shahristani chose in his book called Nihayat al-Iqdam, and there is no doubt that it is closer to the apparent rulings ascribed to the principles of the religion.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage addresses a series of objections and clarifications about internal speech. First, an objection: if God's speech is a particular attribute subsisting in God, then what we read cannot be His speech but rather a likeness; but the consequence is false. If it is a universal, then applying it to a particular is metaphorical. The answer is that the term is equivocal between the universal and the particular. Then the author discusses the relationship between writing, reading, and the eternal speech: writing depicts the wording, and what is read can be either the wording or the meaning depending on intention. The created words and the eternal speech are unified in a way that the word is considered one across all instances. Then the objection about the sequence of letters: the sequence is only in the utterance, not in the uttered word itself. This is defended through a comparison to a motion whose parts are simultaneous in existence. The passage ends by noting that this interpretation, though contrary to later Ash'aris, is chosen by al-Shahristani and is closer to apparent religious rulings.

Key Terms
كلام نفسي
اشتراك
شخص
نوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God's speech is eternal, then the Quran we recite must also be eternal and uncreated.
Correction: The Quran is created as a recited text, but it points to the eternal attribute of speech in God. The term 'speech' is equivocal.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the answer to the objection about God's speech being a particular attribute?
A) It is a universal only.
B) The term is used equivocally.
C) It is a particular only.
D) It is neither.

Key Takeaway
The term 'speech' is equivocal between the eternal attribute and created words, solving the logical dilemma.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you truly believe you are hearing God’s eternal speech, or do your thoughts reduce it to mere human composition? How would that awareness change your concentration today?
?
The objection in this lesson shows that if we insist on a single meaning for 'speech', we end up denying that the recited Quran is truly God's speech. In your own life, where do you insist on simplistic definitions that actually distance you from the reality of God’s attributes?
?
This lesson resolves a logical puzzle, but it also reminds you that God’s speech is both transcendent and intimately accessible in the Quran. Have you ever treated the Quran as just a book of rules, forgetting that every word you read is directly from Him? What will you do to restore that connection starting with your next recitation?
