Written and Recited Speech vs. Eternal Speech
Clarification of written and recited speech in relation to eternal speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how writing and reading relate to the eternal speech of God, and why the recited wording is considered temporal while the meaning is eternal.

Core Idea
Writing and reading are about the wording, but the meaning is one and eternal.

This passage clarifies a subtle distinction: writing depicts the wording, and reading either brings forth the meaning or the wording depending on intention. The recited and memorized words are temporal, yet they refer to the eternal speech. The author explains that the word is considered one across all instances unless distinguished by forms and letters.

Guided Path
1
Understand the terms: writing, reading, wording, meaning
Writing is depiction of the wording with shapes; reading can be of the wording or the meaning. The Mushaf contains the shapes, not the speech itself.
2
Grasp the temporal vs. eternal distinction
The recited wording is temporal, yet the meaning pointed to is eternal. The author says the word is considered one unless distinguished by forms.
This is key to avoiding the error of making the Quran entirely created or entirely eternal.
3
See how this applies to the Quran
Each recitation is a new created act, but the eternal meaning remains the same. The wording is multiple, the meaning is one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فيكون الكلام النفسي عنده أمراً شاملاً للفظ والمعنى اعترض عليه بأن كلام الله تعالى إن كان اسماً لذلك الشخص القائم بذاته تعالى لزم أن لا يكون ما قرأناه كلامه تعالى بل مثله ، واللازم باطل القطع بأن ما يقرأه كل واحد منا وهو الكلام المنزل على النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام بلسان جبرائيل عليه السلام وإن كان اسماً لنوع القائم يلزم أن يكون إطلاقه على ذلك الشخص بخصوصه مجازاً فيجوز نفيه عنه حقيقة، وإن جعل من قبيل كون الموضوع له خاصاً والوضع عاملاً يلزم أن يوصف كلامه تعالى بالحدوث حقيقة أيضاً، وأجيب بالتزام اشتراكه بين النوع وذلك الفرد الخاص .
قوله : (مكتوب) في المصاحف مقروء بالألسن) الكتابة تصوير اللفظ بالنقوش المخصوصة فالثابت في المصحف هو النقوش والمكتوب هو اللفظ والقراءة إن كان ذكر الشيء بلفظه فالمقروء هو المعنى وإن كان ذكر اللفظ فهو اللفظ ثم اللفظ المقروء والمحفوظ الحادث، يعم والقديم بناء على أن اللفظ يعد واحداً في المحال كلها ولا يعتبر متبايناً إلا بتباين الحروف والهيآت .
قوله : ( فجوابه أن ذلك الترتب إلخ ) وقد يقال: القول بأن ترتب الحروف إنما هو في التلفظ دون الملفوظ فالتلفظ حادث دون الملفوظ أمر خارج عن طور العقل وما ذلك إلا مثل أن يتصوّر حركة يكون أجزاؤها مجتمعة في الوجود لا يكون لبعضها تقدم على بعض، ويندفع بما قيل من أن المراد باللفظ اللفظ القائم به تعالى والتلفظ اللفظ القائم بنا عبر عنه بالتلفظ فرقا بينهما
،
وإشعاراً بأن اللفظ الحادث كالنسبة المصدرية لكونه غير قار ولولا هذا الاعتبار لكان القول بقدم

حادث ولا دلة الدلة على الحدوث يجب حملها على حدوثه دون حدوث الملفوظ الأدلة، وهذا الذي ذكرناه وإن كان مخالفا لما عليه متأخرو أصحابنا لا أنه بعد التأمل تعرف حقيقته ثم كلامه وهذا المحمل لكلام الشيخ مما اختاره الشيخ محمد الشهرستاني في كتابه المسمى بنهاية الإقدام ولا شبهة في أنه أقرب إلى الأحكام الظاهرية المنسوبة إلى قواعد الملة .
Commentary: (Regarding his statement: 'so internal speech according to him is something encompassing the wording and the meaning') It was objected against him: If the speech of Allah is a name for that particular [attribute] subsisting in His essence, then it would follow that what we read is not His speech but rather its likeness, and the consequent is false definitely; but if it is a name for a universal of that [attribute], then it would follow that its application to that particular person is metaphorical, so its denial of it would be permissible in reality. And if it is made to be of the type where the thing named is particular and the coinage is general, then it would follow that His speech would be described as temporal in reality as well. And he answered by committing to its being equivocal between the universal and that particular individual. Commentary: (Regarding his statement: 'written in the Mushafs, recited by tongues') Writing is the depiction of the wording with specific shapes. So what is established in the Mushaf is the shapes, and what is written is the wording. As for reading, if it is the mention of the thing by its wording, then what is read is the meaning; and if it is the mention of the wording, then it is the wording. Then the recited and memorized wording is temporal, covering both the universal and the eternal based on the fact that the wording is considered one in all instances and is not considered distinct except by the distinctness of the letters and forms. Commentary: (Regarding his statement: 'so its answer is that this order etc.') And it may be said: The statement that the order of the letters is only in the utterance (talfuz) without the uttered word (malfulz), so the utterance is temporal without the uttered word, is something beyond the scope of reason. That is only like imagining a motion whose parts are simultaneous in existence, none of them being prior to another. And it is repelled by what was said: what is meant by 'the word' is the word subsisting in Him, and 'utterance' is the word subsisting in us; he expressed it as 'utterance' to differentiate between them and to indicate that the temporal word is like an infinitive (al-nisba al-masdariyya) because it is non-permanent. Were it not for this consideration, the statement of the temporal... and the evidence indicating temporality must be interpreted as indicating its temporality, not the temporality of the uttered word. And what we have mentioned, although contrary to the later [view] of our companions, after reflection you will know its truth and then his statement. And this interpretation of the Shaykh's words is what Shaykh Muhammad al-Shahristani chose in his book called Nihayat al-Iqdam, and there is no doubt that it is closer to the apparent rulings ascribed to the principles of the religion.

First, an objection: if God's speech is a particular attribute subsisting in God, then what we read cannot be His speech but rather a likeness; but the consequence is false. The Sequence of Letters in God's Speech
Defense against the objection of sequential letters in eternal speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection about the sequence of letters in God's speech and the defense that the sequence pertains only to the utterance, not to the uttered word.

Core Idea
Letters do not follow one another in God's eternal speech; sequence is in our utterance.

A further objection arises: if God's speech is eternal, how can it consist of letters that come one after another? The author answers by distinguishing between the utterance (talfuz) and the uttered word (malfulz). The sequence is only in the act of pronouncing, not in the eternal word itself. He compares it to an imagined motion whose parts exist simultaneously, and concludes by citing al-Shahristani's endorsement.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection about sequence
The objector says that letters are sequential, so God's speech would be temporal. The author says this is based on a misunderstanding.
2
Learn the distinction between utterance and uttered word
The sequence is in the act of utterance (talfuz), not in the uttered word (malfulz) which is a single entity in God's knowledge.
He uses an analogy of a motion whose parts are simultaneous.
3
Note the supporting authority
The author mentions that this interpretation, though contrary to later Ash'aris, is chosen by al-Shahristani and is closer to apparent religious rulings.
This shows that the view is not mainstream but has scholarly backing.

The Weakness of Denying Attributes Due to Lack of Evidence
Author presents first argument and its logical critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument that 'no evidence means no attribute' is logically flawed, and how the concepts of malzum and lazim apply.

Core Idea
Absence of evidence is not evidence of absence in theology.

The author introduces the question of whether God has additional attributes beyond the seven. He presents the first position from those who deny such attributes solely because there is no evidence for them. The author immediately critiques this reasoning, exposing its logical weakness. We will explore this critique and the logical principles he uses.

Guided Path
1
Read the preface and first position
Read the opening lines of the passage where the author poses the question and the first group argues that lack of evidence requires negation.
Notice that the group confines itself to 'no evidence' as the sole reason.
2
Understand the author's critique
The author states that the absence of evidence does not prove non-existence in reality. Reflect on why this is a common fallacy even today.
This is the key teaching point: absence of evidence is not evidence of absence.
3
Examine the logical principle
The author explains that the non-existence of a conditional (malzum) does not entail the non-existence of its necessary condition (lazim). Carefully follow the commentary that unpacks this logic.
The commentary provides two interpretations. Focus on the main point: disproving the conditional does not disprove its necessary consequence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الثامن : في أنه تعالى صفة ( في صفات اختلف فيها وفيه مقدمة ومسائل) إحدى عشرة ( فالمقدمة : ) هي أنه ( هل لله تعالى صفة ) وجودية زائدة على ذاته ( غير ما ذكرناه ) من الصفات السبع الحياة والعلم والقدرة والإرادة والسمع والبصر والكلام، فمنعه بعض التي هي أصحابنا مقتصراً) في نفيها ( على أنه لا دليل عليه ) أي على ثبوت صفة أخرى ( فيجب نفيه ولا يخفى ضعفه لما مر من أن عدم الدليل عندك لا يفيد عدمه في ) نفس الأمر ممنوع وإن سلم لم يفد أيضا لأن انتفاء الملزوم لا يستلزم انتفاء لازمه، ( ومنهم من زاد على ذلك فاستدل على نفيها بأن ) قال : نحن مكلفون بكمال المعرفة ) وإنما يحصل بمعرفة جميع صفاته ( فلو كان له صفة غيرها لعرفناها ) لكنا لا نعرفها بل لا طريق لنا إلى معرفة الصفات سوى الاستدلال بالأفعال والتنزيه عن النقائص، ولا

قوله : ( لأن انتفاء الملزوم لا يستلزم انتفاء (لازمه) يحتمل أن يحمل على حذف المضاف أي لا يستلزم انتفاء متعلق لازمه وذلك لأن المراد هاهنا نفي لزوم انتفاء ثبوت الصفة، ولا شك أن الدليل على تقدير وجوده يستلزم التصديق بذلك الثبوت ويحتمل أن يبقى على ظاهره لأن الدليل كالعالم قد يستلزم نفس المدلول كوجود الصانع وإن لم يكن محصلا له في الواقع، وعلى كل تقدير لا أن انتفاء ملزوم خاص وإن لم يستلزم انتفاء اللازم مطلقاً إلا أن انتفاء الملزوم يرد بالكلية يستلزم انتفاء اللازم فيلزم انتفاء صفة لم يقم عليها دليل أما على التقدير الأول فظاهر وأما على التقدير الثاني فلأنه لا يلزم من نفي الملزوم الذي لا مدخل له في حصول لازمة نفي اللازم كما مر في جواب تاسع شبه السمنية على فائدة النظر العلم .

قوله : ( ومنهم من زاد على ذلك ليس المراد بالزيادة الزيادة بحسب الكمية بأن يقولوا بما قاله الأولون مع زيادة إذ لا معنى له قطعاً بل الزيادة بحسب الكيفية وهي القوة، وسيجيء نظيره في بحث أن الإيمان هل يزيد وينقص .
The eighth objective: Concerning that He, exalted, is an attribute (Among the attributes about which there is disagreement, and it includes a preface and eleven issues). (The preface:) It is that (Does Allah, exalted, have an existential attribute additional to His essence) (other than what we mentioned) of the seven attributes: life, knowledge, power, will, hearing, seeing, and speech. Some of our companions forbade it, confining themselves in negating it to (that there is no evidence for it), i.e., for the existence of another attribute, (so it must be negated). And its weakness is not hidden because of what has been mentioned: that the absence of evidence for you does not prove its non-existence in reality; it is prevented, and even if granted, it is not useful because the non-existence of the conditional (malzum) does not entail the non-existence of its necessary condition (lazim). (And among them is one who added to that and argued for its negation by) saying: We are obligated to perfect knowledge, and that is only attained by knowing all His attributes. So if He had an attribute other than them, we would know it, but we do not know it; rather we have no way to know the attributes except by inference from actions and transcendence above deficiencies. And he said... Commentary: (Regarding his statement: 'because the non-existence of the conditional does not entail the non-existence of its necessary condition') It can be interpreted by ellipsis of the genitive construction, i.e., it does not entail the non-existence of the object of its necessary condition. That is because what is meant here is the negation of the necessary entailment of the non-existence of the existence of the attribute. And there is no doubt that the evidence, if it exists, entails affirmation of that existence. And it can be left apparent, because evidence, like the world, may entail the very thing signified, such as the existence of the Creator, even if it does not produce it in reality. On either assessment, the non-existence of a particular conditional, even if it does not entail the non-existence of the necessary condition absolutely, yet the non-existence of the conditional in totality entails the non-existence of the necessary condition, so it would entail the non-existence of an attribute for which no evidence exists. As for the first interpretation, it is apparent; as for the second, because the negation of the conditional that has no part in the occurrence of its necessary condition does not entail the negation of the necessary condition, as has been mentioned in the answer to the ninth sophistry of the Sumaniyya on the benefit of theoretical consideration. Commentary: (Regarding his statement: 'And among them is one who added to that') What is meant by 'addition' is not addition in terms of quantity, so that they would say what the first ones said with an addition, for that is meaningless absolutely. Rather, the addition is in terms of quality, meaning strength. And its equivalent will come in the discussion of whether faith increases and decreases.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage begins the eighth chapter on God's attributes, with a preface and eleven issues. The preface asks whether God has additional existential attributes beyond the seven mentioned (life, knowledge, power, will, hearing, seeing, speech). The first position: some of our companions deny additional attributes, arguing only that there is no evidence for them, so they must be negated. The author points out the weakness of this: the absence of evidence for you does not prove absence in reality. Even if granted, negation of the necessary condition does not entail negation of its consequence. Then a stronger argument from the same group: we are obligated to perfect knowledge, which requires knowing all attributes; if there were additional attributes, we would know them, but we do not. The author then comments on the logical point that the absence of a condition (malzum) does not entail the absence of its necessary consequence (lazim) in all cases, distinguishing between particular and universal negation. The gloss also clarifies that when some 'added' an argument, it is a qualitative addition, not quantitative.

وجودي
زائد
ملزوم
لازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Lack of evidence for a divine attribute proves the attribute does not exist.
Correction: The author shows that absence of evidence does not prove absence in reality; it is a logical fallacy. The non-existence of a conditional does not entail the non-existence of its necessary condition.

Checkpoint
What is the main weakness the author identifies in the first argument that denies additional attributes based on lack of evidence?
A) The evidence is actually present.
B) Absence of evidence does not prove absence.
C) The seven attributes are sufficient.
D) The argument contradicts the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The author argues that the absence of evidence does not constitute evidence for non-existence, and the logical relationship between a conditional and its necessary condition must be carefully understood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever quietly doubted an attribute of Allah—like His mercy in your suffering or His justice in the face of evil—because you couldn't see evidence of it in your circumstances? How does this lesson expose the hidden arrogance of requiring evidence before accepting what Allah has told you? Will you now commit to affirming His attributes even when your limited vision cannot trace them?
?
Think of a specific decision you made recently where you assumed something was false just because you lacked proof. Could this fallacy have crept into your relationship with Allah? Where might you be denying His truth because your own 'evidence' is missing? What would it cost you to submit to revelation even without personal verification?
?
This lesson teaches that absence of evidence is not evidence of absence. How many of Allah's names and attributes might you have unwittingly negated by silence or neglect because you never encountered a 'proof' that satisfied your intellect? Will you now actively seek to affirm what He has revealed, trusting that His speech is evidence enough, even when your mind cannot grasp the 'how'?

The Stronger Argument: Perfect Knowledge Requires Knowing All Attributes
Author presents a stronger argument against additional attributes and clarifies the nature of the addition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second, stronger argument against additional attributes: that our obligation to perfect knowledge requires us to know all of God's attributes, and since we do not know additional ones, they do not exist.

Core Idea
If we must know all attributes, then unknown attributes cannot exist.

After critiquing the first argument, the author presents a second, more robust argument from the same group. This argument is based on the obligation to attain perfect cognition of God, which would require knowing all attributes. Since we have no way to know additional attributes, they must not exist. The author also clarifies that this addition is qualitative (strength), not quantitative.

Guided Path
1
Read the second argument
Read the passage starting from 'And among them is one who added to that'. Understand the reasoning: we are obligated to perfect knowledge, which requires knowing all attributes; if there were more, we would know them; but we do not, so there are no more.
Notice this argument is different because it uses an epistemological premise.
2
Analyze the logic
The argument assumes that perfect knowledge is possible and that we would have access to all attributes. Consider whether this assumption holds according to Islamic theology.
This relates to the scope of human knowledge about God.
3
Understand the clarification about 'addition'
The author (in commentary) explains that when they said 'added to that', it is a qualitative addition (strength of argument), not just another argument of the same kind.
This helps avoid misunderstanding the group's position.

The First Additional Attribute: Al-Baqa'
States that some theologians affirm additional attributes and introduces the attribute of al-baqa'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that theologians affirm additional attributes of Allah and that the first among them is al-baqa', and the dispute over whether it is a positive attribute additional to essence.

Core Idea
Al-baqa' (eternal existence) may be a separate positive attribute, not just a negation.

This lesson begins the discussion of additional divine attributes. The author notes that some theologians affirm attributes beyond the essence, and he enumerates eleven issues. The first issue is al-baqa', or eternal existence. While all agree that Allah is eternal, they differ on whether baqa' is a positive attribute added to existence. The passage introduces the position of al-Ash'ari and the Mu'tazila of Baghdad who affirm it as a separate attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then its English translation. Notice the agreement on Allah's eternity but the dispute on baqa' as an attribute.
The text is incomplete; it cuts off at 'إذ'.

These terms are central to the debate.
3
Reflect on the dispute
Consider why some theologians might see baqa' as a separate attribute while others do not. What could be the argument for it being additional?
The passage hints that the argument continues on the next page.

Obligation to Know God's Attributes According to Capacity
Refutation of objection by clarifying obligation is capacity-based.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that the obligation to know God's attributes is proportionate to human capacity, and that complete knowledge is not required of all.

Core Idea
Obligation depends on capacity, not on complete knowledge.

In this passage, the author addresses an objection about the necessity of knowing additional attributes of God. He responds by clarifying that the obligation to know is not absolute but is measured by what is necessary for faith and what individuals can bear. This subtly distinguishes between the requirements for ordinary believers and the perfect knowledge of prophets.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without worrying about the parentheses. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the objection and the two answers presented.
2
Identify the objection
Find the part where an opposing view is mentioned: that nothing indicates an additional attribute beyond what was already covered.
The objection is implied at the beginning before 'والجواب'.
3
Understand the first answer
The first answer denies the obligation of complete knowledge; rather, obligation is according to capacity. We are only required to know what is necessary for believing the prophet.
This limits the scope of knowledge required.
4
Understand the second answer
The second answer grants the obligation of complete knowledge but argues that not everyone attains it—only prophets and their perfect followers do.
This explains why many may perish without attaining complete knowledge.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this argument defends the necessity of knowing some attributes while allowing for varying levels of knowledge among believers.
The author maintains that perishing due to neglect is possible but not contradictory.

The Fourth Observatory - The Eighth Topic: That He, exalted, is an attribute. Something from both indicates an additional attribute beyond what was mentioned. (The answer: denial of the obligation of complete knowledge of Him, for it) i.e., the obligation (is according to our capacity). So we are obligated to know from His attributes that which belief in the prophet, peace be upon him, depends on knowing, not to know other attributes. (Or) by saying: we grant our obligation to know Him completely, but it does not follow from the obligation that it is attained by all who are obligated. Rather, perhaps (some know Him) with complete knowledge, such as the prophets and the perfect among their followers, (and not) others besides them. And these, even if they are the majority, (yet) (it is not impossible that many perish due to abandoning what they were obligated of complete knowledge of Him).
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The objection is that nothing from the earlier discussion indicates an additional attribute beyond what was already mentioned. The author responds by denying the obligation of complete knowledge; rather, obligation is according to capacity. The author then elaborates two possibilities: either we are only obligated to know attributes necessary for believing the prophet, or even if we are obligated to complete knowledge, it is not necessary that all attain it—some (prophets and their perfect followers) do, while others do not, and it is not impossible that many perish for neglecting that obligation. The commentary emphasizes the limits of human knowledge and the possibility of perishing.

Proof That Eternity Is an Additional Positive Attribute
The author presents the view that baqa' is a positive attribute and defends it against the objection of huduth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that baqa' is an additional positive attribute and how the objection from huduth is resolved.

Core Idea
Change in description does not always imply a new positive attribute; sometimes it is merely negational.

In this passage, the author examines whether eternity (baqa') is a distinct positive attribute added to existence. Some theologians affirm it based on the fact that existence can be realized without eternity, as in the beginning of temporal things. However, an objection arises using the concept of temporality (huduth), which if treated similarly would also imply it is positive. The author resolves by clarifying that changes in description can involve negational attributes, not necessarily positive ones.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation to understand the flow of the argument.
Notice the compressed style and embedded refutations.
2
Identify the thesis and its proof
The thesis is that baqa' is an additional positive attribute. The proof: existence can be realized without baqa' (as at the beginning of temporality).
The proof uses the idea of 'realized without' to argue for extra.
3
Examine the objection
The objector uses huduth (temporality) as a counterexample: if the same reasoning holds, huduth would also be an additional attribute, which is not accepted.
This objection also raises the threat of infinite regress.
4
Understand the resolution
The author responds that renewal of description (e.g., becoming eternal) does not necessitate a positive attribute; it can be a negational attribute.
The example of 'renewal of accompaniment' illustrates this.
5
Reflect on the key insight
Consider how this argument preserves the doctrine that baqa' is a positive attribute while avoiding the unwanted consequence for huduth.
This shows the sophistication of kalam reasoning.

Source Text

وأثبت بعض من المتكلمين صفات أخر بيانها تلك المسائل الإحدى عشرة ( الأولى البقاء) اتفقوا على أنه تعالى باق لكن اخلتفوا في كونه صفة ثبوتية زائدة كما أشار إليه بقوله : ( أثبته الشيخ أبو الحسن وأتباعه وجمهور معتزلة بغداد (صفة) وجودية (زائد على الوجود إذ الوجود متحقق دونه أي دون البقاء (كما في أول الحدوث، بل يتجدد بعده صفة هي البقاء ( وأجيب عنه بأنه منقوض بالحدوث فإنه غير الوجود لتحقق الوجود بعد الحدوث ) يعني أن البقاء حصل بعدما لم يكن والحدوث زال بعد أن كان لأنه الخروج من العدم إلى الوجود عند الشيخ لا مسبوقية الوجود به ( فلو دل ذلك ) الذي ذكرتموه في البقاء على كونه وجودياً زائداً لكان الحدوث أيضاً وجودياً زائداً ) لما ذكرناه لأن العدم بعد الحصول كالحصول بعد العدم في الدلالة على الوجود في الجملة، إذ حاصلهما الانتقال الواقع بين العدم وما يقابله أعني الوجود ( ولزم التسلسل ) في الحدوثات الوجودية ضرورة أن الحدوث لا بد أن يكون حادثاً أن الشيخ معترف بأن الحدوث ليس أمراً زائداً، وحله بعد نقضه أن تجدد الاتصاف بصفة لا تقتضي كونها وجودية كتجدد معية الباري تعالى مع وكذا زواله أيضاً لا يقتضيه، وذلك كله لجواز اتصافه بالعدميات وزوال ذلك الاتصاف
And some of the theologians affirmed other attributes, their explanation are those eleven issues. (The first: Eternity). They agreed that He, exalted, is eternal, but they differed on whether it is an additional positive attribute, as he indicated by his saying: (The Sheikh Abu al-Hasan and his followers and the majority of the Mu'tazila of Baghdad affirmed it as) a positive attribute (additional to existence, since existence is realized without it, i.e., without eternity, as at the beginning of temporality, but rather after it an attribute which is eternity arises. (And it was answered that it is refuted by temporality, for it is other than existence because existence is realized after temporality.) Meaning that eternity came into being after it was not, and temporality ceased after it was, because according to the Sheikh it is the exit from non-existence to existence, not the priority of existence by it. (So if that which you mentioned about eternity indicates that it is additional and positive, then temporality would also be additional and positive) for what we mentioned, because non-existence after existence is like existence after non-existence in indicating existence in general, since both are the transition between non-existence and its opposite, i.e., existence. (And an infinite regress would follow) in positive temporalities, necessarily because temporality must be temporal, whereas the Sheikh admits that temporality is not an additional thing. And its resolution after refuting it is that renewal of description by an attribute does not necessitate that it be positive, like the renewal of the accompaniment of the Creator, exalted, with... and likewise its cessation does not necessitate it. And all that is due to the permissibility of His being described by negational attributes and the cessation of that description.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage presents the affirmative view that some theologians (Ash'aris and Baghdad Mu'tazila) consider baqa' (eternity) a positive attribute additional to existence. The proof: existence can be realized without baqa' (as at the beginning of a temporal thing), so baqa' is an added attribute. An objection is raised: this argument is refuted by the example of huduth (temporality), which is not an additional attribute. The response: if the same logic applied, huduth would also be additional, but huduth is simply the transition from non-existence to existence, not a positive attribute. The author resolves by stating that a change in description (e.g., something becoming eternal or ceasing) does not imply a positive attribute; it could be a negational attribute. The reference to 'renewal of accompaniment' illustrates that change can occur without a new positive attribute existing in the subject. The argument also warns of infinite regress if every attribute required another attribute to subsist.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if eternity arises after non-existence, it must be a positive attribute like other temporal accidents.
Correction: The author shows that this reasoning is not necessary, as change can be described in negational terms.

Checkpoint
What objection is raised against the proof that baqa' is an additional positive attribute?
A) That baqa' is identical to existence
B) That huduth is also additional if the same logic is applied
C) That infinite regress is impossible

Key Takeaway
Eternity's status as an additional positive attribute is defended by showing that change in description does not necessitate a new positive attribute.

The objector used flawed reasoning to challenge the truth. Denial of Eternity as an Additional Attribute: The Regress Proof
The author states the opposing view that baqa' is identical to existence and gives the first proof (regress).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first rational proof against the view that baqa' (eternity) is an additional attribute over and above existence, known as the regress argument.

Core Idea
If eternity were an extra attribute, it would itself need eternity, leading to an infinite regress.

In this passage, the author presents the position of several major theologians who deny that God's attribute of eternity (baqa') is a separate, positive attribute added to His existence. They argue that baqa' is identical to existence in the second moment of time. Their first proof is a powerful regress argument: if baqa' were additional, it would require its own eternity, creating an infinite chain. This lesson examines that argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proponents
Read the names of the scholars who held this view: Qadi Abu Bakr, Imam al-Haramayn, Imam al-Razi, and the Basran Mu'tazila. Why are these authorities significant?
2
Understand the core claim
The claim is that baqa' is 'the same as existence in the second moment' (nafs al-wujud fi al-zaman al-thani). What does it mean for an attribute to be identical to existence?
This is a strong realist position.
3
Analyze the regress argument
The proof states: if baqa' were additional, it would need its own baqa' to be eternal, leading to infinite regress. Work through this logic step by step.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the second proof is not given here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونفاه القاضي أبو بكر والإمامان إمام الحرمين والإمام الرازي وجمهور معتزلة البصرة ، وقالوا : البقاء هو نفس الوجود في الزمان الثاني ( لا أمر زائد عليه لوجهين : الأول ( لو كان ( البقاء ( زائداً لكان له بقاء ) إذ لو لم يكن البقاء باقياً لم يكن الوجود باقياً، لأن كونه باقياً إنما هو بواسطة البقاء
And it was denied by Qadi Abu Bakr and the two Imams, Imam al-Haramayn and Imam al-Razi, and the majority of the Mu'tazila of Basra. And they said: Eternity is the same as existence in the second moment (not an additional thing, for two reasons: The first: (If eternity were additional, it would have its own eternity). For if eternity were not eternal, existence would not be eternal, because its being eternal is only by means of eternity.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage presents the opposing view that baqa' is not an additional positive attribute. The prominent figures holding this view are Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani, Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni, Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, and the Basran Mu'tazila. They argue that baqa' is identical to existence in the second moment of time. The first proof (of two) is a regress argument: if baqa' were additional, it would itself need to be eternal (baqi), requiring another baqa' to make it eternal, leading to an infinite regress. The passage as preserved ends before completing the second proof. The implicit conclusion is that such regress is impossible, so baqa' cannot be additional.

إمام الرازي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If baqa' is not an additional attribute, then God's attribute of eternity does not exist.
Correction: The view does not deny eternity; it identifies it with the continuation of existence itself. Eternity is real but not a separate entity from existence.

Checkpoint

D) That the prophets know God's attributes completely

Key Takeaway
The first proof against baqa' being an additional attribute is that it would require its own baqa' to be eternal, leading to an impossible infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Consider a time when you sought security in something other than God. How does the reality that God's eternity is not a separate attribute but His very existence continuing—without any addition—challenge your tendency to add 'saviors' alongside Him?
?
The proof shows that adding an attribute leads to absurdity. In your own life, where have you been 'adding' to God's perfection by assuming He needs your efforts or rituals to sustain His reign? What would it mean to let go of that assumption today?

The First Proof Against Baqa' as Additional
Denial of baqa' as additional and first proof via infinite regress

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof against baqa' being an additional attribute and how it leads to an infinite regress.

Core Idea
If baqa' were additional, it would need its own baqa', causing an infinite chain.

The famous theologian al-Qadi Abu Bakr and others argue that baqa' is not a separate attribute but identical to existence in the second moment. They present a powerful proof: if baqa' were additional, it would require its own permanence, leading to an impossible infinite regress. Let's examine this argument in detail.

Guided Path
1
Identify the authorities and claim
Read the opening line to see who denies baqa' as additional and what they affirm instead.
Note that al-Qadi Abu Bakr, al-Juwayni, al-Razi, and the Basran Mu'tazila are mentioned.
2
Understand the conditional logic
Focus on the conditional statement: if baqa' were additional, it would have a baqa'. Consider why this is necessary.
Because anything that is existent and permanent requires its own permanence.
3
Grasp the infinite regress
Follow the reasoning: if the supposed baqa' were not permanent, existence would not be permanent; so it must be permanent, but then its permanence requires another baqa', leading to an infinite series.
The regress involves simultaneous existence of multiple baqa' attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونفاه القاضي أبو بكر والإمامان إمام الحرمين والإمام الرازي وجمهور معتزلة البصرة، وقالوا: البقاء هو نفس الوجود في الزمان الثاني لا أمر زائد عليه لوجهين: الأول: لو كان البقاء زائداً لكان له بقاء، إذ لو لم يكن البقاء باقياً لم يكن الوجود باقياً، لأن كونه باقياً إنما هو بواسطة البقاء، فالبقاء المفروض زواله، وحينئذ تتسلسل البقاءات المترتبة الموجودة معاً، والجواب: إن بقاء البقاء نفس البقاء كما قيل في وجود الوجود، ووجوب الوجوب، وإمكان الإمكان فلا تسلسل أصلا، ويرد على هذا الجواب أن ما تكرر نوعه يجب كونه اعتبارياً كما مر.
And it was denied by al-Qadi Abu Bakr, the two Imams (Imam al-Haramayn and Imam al-Razi), and the majority of the Basran Mu'tazila. They said: al-baqā' (permanence) is the same as existence in the second moment, not an additional thing, for two reasons. The first: if baqā' were additional, it would have a baqā'. For if baqā' were not permanent, existence would not be permanent, because its being permanent is only through baqā'. So the supposed baqā' would cease, and then there would be an infinite regress of successive and simultaneously existing baqā'āt. The answer: indeed baqā' of baqā' is the same as baqā', as is said about existence of existence, necessity of necessity, and possibility of possibility; so there is no regress at all. But it is objected to this answer that whatever repeats in kind must be mentally-posited (iʿtibārī), as has passed.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof from those who deny that baqā' (permanence) is an additional existent attribute distinct from existence. It begins by naming the authorities: al-Qadi Abu Bakr (al-Baqillani), Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni), Imam al-Razi, and the Basran Mu'tazila. They assert that baqā' is identical to existence in the second moment. The first proof is: if baqā' were an additional attribute, it would itself need to be permanent (i.e., have its own baqā'). Otherwise, if baqā' were not permanent, then existence would not be permanent either—because existence's permanence is through baqā'. But then the supposed baqā' would be subject to cessation, leading to an infinite regress of successive baqā' attributes existing simultaneously. The response to this argument is that baqā' of baqā' is the same as baqā' itself, just as 'existence of existence' is the same as existence, 'necessity of necessity' the same as necessity, etc. Thus no regress occurs. However, a counter-objection is raised: if a type (like baqā') repeats in kind, it must be mentally-posited (iʿtibārī), not a real existent. This principle undercuts the response and reopens the regress problem. The passage thus presents a self-contained argument with an objection and a rebuttal, showing the complexity of the debate.

Key Terms
بقاء
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Infinite regress is automatically impossible without further reasoning.
Correction: The proof shows that an infinite regress of simultaneously existing attributes is impossible, but it is a specific kind of regress.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, if baqa' were an additional attribute, what would it require?
A) It would be eternal.
B) It would have its own baqa'.
C) It would be identical to existence.
D) It would be a mental concept.

Key Takeaway
The first proof uses the necessity of baqa' for any permanent existent to generate an infinite regress, thereby denying baqa' as an additional attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that if we ascribe permanence as a separate thing, we fall into an endless chain. In your own life, what 'additions' do you rely on for stability—money, status, relationships—that are not self-sustaining? How does this truth challenge you to anchor your sense of permanence in Allah alone, whose existence requires no support?
?
The argument forces us to confront the necessity of baqā' being intrinsic to existence. When you consider your own fleeting moments, how does it affect the way you live each second? Are you living as if your existence in the next moment is guaranteed by something other than the One whose permanence is inherent? What must change today?

Answer and Counter-Objection to the Regress
Response and counter-objection to the infinite regress proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the response to the infinite regress argument and the counter-objection that repeating types imply mentally-posited existence.

Core Idea
The answer that baqa' of baqa' is baqa' itself is countered by the principle of i'tibari attributes.

The argument against baqa' being additional seems compelling, but its proponents offer a rebuttal: baqa' of baqa' is simply baqa' itself, analogous to existence of existence. However, a further objection arises: if a concept repeats in kind, it must be mentally-posited, not real. This passage explores this critical exchange.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the answer
Read the answer: 'baqa' of baqa' is the same as baqa''. Notice the analogy with existence of existence, etc.
This is a self-reflexive identity claim.
2
Recognize the reasoning
The answer claims that no regress occurs because the second-order attribute is identical to the first-order one.
3
Understand the counter-objection
The objection states that anything that repeats in kind must be i'tibari (mentally-posited). This undermines the answer because it implies baqa' would not be real.
Refer to the earlier discussion on i'tibari attributes.

The Circularity Argument Against Baqā'
The second proof against baqā' as additional and the commentator's remarks.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof that baqā' cannot be an additional attribute because it would create circular dependence between baqā' and the divine essence.

Core Idea
If baqā' were additional, the essence and baqā' would each need the other—an impossible circle.

The second proof against baqā' being an existent attribute is a tight logical argument: if baqā' is additional, it must depend on the essence, but the essence also depends on baqā' for its continuation. This circularity shows the impossibility. The commentator adds a nuance about the renewal of baqā' and a methodological note.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof and its conclusion
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice the conditional structure: if baqā' needs essence, then circle. Also note the alternative: if baqā' does not need essence, then essence needs it while baqā' is independent.
The proof has two branches both leading to impossibility.
2
Understand the commentator's restriction
The commentator (Sanusi) suggests that if the proof is granted, it should be restricted to the implication that renewal of baqā' makes God a locus of accidents, which is a different issue.
This shows the proof is not conclusive as stated.
3
Reflect on the final note about circularity
The final sentence points out that one cannot claim baqā' needs essence absolutely and vice versa—an incomplete thought but hints at a response.
Consider why this objection might fail.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: لو احتاج البقاء على تقدير كونه وجودياً إلى الذات لزم الدور، لأن الذات محتاج إلى البقاء أيضاً، فإن وجوده في الزمان الثاني معلل به، وإلا لكان الذات محتاجاً إليه وكان مستغنياً. قلت: لو سلم كان ينبغي أن يقصر على أن تجدد البقاء يستلزم أن يكون تعالى محل الحوادث، ويمكن أن يقال: إنه من باب تعيين الطريق. قوله: (والجواب أن بقاء البقاء نفس البقاء) فيه بحث: أما أولاً فلأنه يجوز حينئذ أن يكون بقاء الباري تعالى أيضاً نفسه وإثبات أن البقاء لا يحتاج في البقاء إلى بقاء زائد بخلاف غيره مشكل، وأما ثانياً فلأن الخصم القائل بكون البقاء صفة وجودية زائدة على الوجود لا يمكنه هذا الجواب ولا القول بجواز أن يكون بقاء البقاء مثلاً اعتبارياً كما سبق في نظائره، وذلك لأن دليله الذي استدل به على كون البقاء صفة وجودية زائدة على الوجود يدل على كون بقاء البقاء صفة وجودية زائدة على البقاء إذ البقاء يتحقق بدونه كما في ثاني زمان الحدوث، ويتجدد بعده صفة بقاء البقاء، والحاصل أن دليل النافي يتم إلزاماً وإن لم يتم برهاناً. قوله: (ويرد على هذا الجواب إلخ) فيه بحث أشرنا إليه في الأمور العامة وهو أن هذا الجواب يرد على قولهم: ما تكرر نوعه إلخ لأن هذا القول يرد على ذلك الجواب، وتوضيحه أن هذا القول ضابطة ذكرها صاحب التلويحات وبينها بأنها لو لم يكن اعتبارياً لزم التسلسل في الأمور الموجودة المترتبة، وإذا منع لزوم التسلسل يكون بقاء البقاء مثلاً عين البقاء لا فكيف يثبت بها ما هو المطلوب أعني كون البقاء أمراً اعتبارياً، نعم لو ثبت اعتراف المثبت بوجودية البقاء بتلك القاعدة لأمكن توجيه الإيراد بأن خلاصته هو أن ادعاءهم وجودية البقاء وانقطاع التسلسل بكون بقائه عينه يرده اعترافهم بأن ما تكرر نوعه يجب أن يكون اعتبارياً سواء تم دليله أم لا، ويمكن أن يجعل لفظ يرد على صيغة المجهول من الرد لا على صيغة المعلوم من الورود ويجعل أن ما تكرر نوعه إلخ فاعله فحينئذ يكون إعادة لما ذكره في الأمور العامة ويكون فائدة الإعادة دفع سؤال متوهم وهو أنه كيف يدعي وجودية البقاء وكون بقائه عينه أنه قد سابق أن ما تكرر نوعه يجب كونه اعتبارياً، فأشار إلى دفعه بأن تلك القاعدة مردودة بهذا الجواب، وعلى هذا أيضاً يندفع البحث لكن يرد عليه بأن هذا الجواب يرد دليل ذلك المدعى ورد الدليل لا يكون رداً للمدعي لأن إبطال الملزوم لا يفيد إبطال اللازم، سيما والمستفاد من الدليل المذكور العلم بالمدلول فاللازم من إبطاله انتفاء ذلك العلم لا انتفاء نفس المدلول، اللهم إلا أن يريد بالرد مجرد عدم ثبوته لا بطلانه في نفسه فإن ذلك القدر يكفي هاهنا لدفع ذلك السؤال فليتأمل. قوله: (لزم الدور) لا يقال: احتياج البقاء إلى الذات بالنسبة إلى وجودها مطلقاً وعكسه
The second: if baqā', on the assumption of its being existent, needs the essence, then circularity is necessary, because the essence also needs baqā', since its existence in the second moment is caused by it. Otherwise, the essence would be needful of it while it (baqā') would be independent. I say: if this is granted, it should be restricted to the fact that the renewal of baqā' entails that the Exalted is a locus of accidents; and it may be said that this is a matter of specifying the way. His statement: (And the answer that baqā' of baqā' is the same as baqā') is debatable. First, because then it would be permissible that the baqā' of the Exalted Himself is also the same, and proving that baqā' does not need a further baqā' unlike others is problematic. Second, because the opponent who holds baqā' to be an additional existent attribute cannot accept this answer nor the claim that baqā' of baqā' is mentally-posited, as in similar cases, because his proof for baqā' being additional also proves that baqā' of baqā' is additional, since baqā' is realized without it as in the second moment of origination, and afterwards the attribute of baqā' of baqā' is renewed. The upshot is that the denier's argument is conclusive as an objection even if not as a proof. His statement: (And it is objected to this answer etc.) has a discussion we referred to in the General Matters, namely that this answer refutes their statement 'whatever repeats in kind etc.' because that statement refutes the answer. The explanation is that this statement is a principle mentioned by the author of al-Talwīḥāt, and he explained that if it were not mentally-posited, there would be infinite regress in successive existent things. If infinite regress is prevented, then baqā' of baqā' would be identical to baqā', so how can the desired point — that baqā' is mentally-posited — be established by it? Yes, if the affirmant's admission of baqā' being existent by that principle were established, the objection could be directed such that its gist is: their claim of baqā' being existent and the cessation of regress by identifying it with baqā' itself is refuted by their admission that whatever repeats in kind must be mentally-posited, whether its proof is sound or not. And the word 'yuriddu' (is refuted) can be made passive of 'radda' rather than active of 'warada', and 'that which repeats in kind etc.' can be made its subject; then it would be a repetition of what was mentioned in the General Matters, and the benefit of repetition is to repel a hypothetical question: how can he claim existence of baqā' and that its baqā' is identical to it when it was previously established that whatever repeats in kind must be mentally-posited? So he indicated its repulsion by saying that that principle is refuted by this answer. And with this, the investigation is also repelled, but it is objected that this answer refutes the proof of that claim, and refuting the proof is not a refutation of the claimer, because invalidating the necessary does not invalidate the necessary consequence, especially since what is derived from that proof is knowledge of the conclusion; the necessary consequence of its invalidation is the absence of that knowledge, not the absence of the conclusion itself, unless by 'refutation' he means mere lack of proof, not its falsity in itself, for that much suffices here to repel the question. So let it be considered. His statement: (Circularity is necessary) cannot be said: the need of baqā' for the essence is with respect to its existence absolutely, and vice versa.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof against baqā' being an additional attribute and then engages in a lengthy critical analysis of the responses. The second proof is an argument from circularity: if baqā' (on the assumption that it is an existent attribute) needs the essence (i.e., depends on the divine essence for its existence), then there is circularity because the essence itself needs baqā' for its continued existence in the second moment. If, on the other hand, baqā' does not need the essence, then the essence would be needful of baqā' while baqā' is independent, which is also problematic. The commentator then interjects a remark that if this reasoning is granted, it should be limited to the point that the renewal of baqā' would imply that the Exalted is a locus of accidents — a different issue. Next, the discussion turns to the response that 'baqā' of baqā' is the same as baqā''. This response is criticized on two fronts: first, it would allow that God's own baqā' is identical to Himself, which is problematic; second, the opponent who holds baqā' to be an additional attribute cannot use this response because his own reasoning would force him to say that baqā' of baqā' is also additional, leading to an infinite regress. The passage then delves into the principle 'whatever repeats in kind must be mentally-posited', attributed to the author of al-Talwīḥāt. There is a debate about whether this principle refutes the response or is itself refuted by it. The commentator suggests that the word 'yuriddu' can be read as passive, turning the principle into a repetition from an earlier discussion, used to answer a hypothetical question. However, a further objection arises: refuting the proof does not refute the claim itself unless 'refutation' is understood as merely showing the lack of proof. The passage ends with a final note on the circularity argument, stating that one cannot claim that baqā' needs the essence in an absolute sense while vice versa also holds — presumably an incomplete thought. Overall, this is a dense dialectical passage exploring multiple layers of objection and response.

Key Terms
دور
بقاء
حوادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the circularity argument perfectly disproves baqā' as additional.
Correction: The commentator shows the proof is not conclusive: it only works if we ignore the alternative that the renewal of baqā' makes God a locus of accidents, which is a separate issue.

Checkpoint
What does the second proof conclude?
A) Baqā' must be eternal.
B) If baqā' needs essence and essence needs baqā', circularity arises.
C) Baqā' is identical to existence.
D) Baqā' is a mental positing.

Key Takeaway
The second proof attempts to show that an additional baqā' leads to circular dependence, but the commentator notes it is not conclusive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own beliefs about God's attributes, do you ever catch yourself using circular reasoning—'God is eternal because He must be eternal'—without grounding it in divine revelation? What does this lesson teach you about the danger of relying solely on human logic in matters of faith?
?
The commentator's restriction shows that even a seemingly strong argument may have hidden assumptions. In your own life, where have you held a theological conviction so tightly that you ignored its limitations? Will you now examine that conviction with the same critical humility?
?
If the certainty of God's continued existence (baqā') does not come from rational proof but from trust in revelation, then what in your daily life depends on that trust? How would your actions change if you truly believed that every moment of your existence is sustained directly by God's will, not by an abstract attribute?

The Response: Baqā' of Baqā' Is Identical
Presentation and critique of the response 'baqā' of baqā' identical to baqā''.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the response that 'baqā' of baqā' is identical to baqā'' and the two objections raised against it.

Core Idea
The response that baqā' of baqā' is identical fails because it leads to problems for both sides.

One proposed answer to the infinite regress problem is that the baqā' of baqā' is the same as baqā' itself. However, this answer is criticized on two fronts: it would allow God's own baqā' to be identical to Himself, and it is not available to the opponent who holds baqā' to be additional, because his own reasoning would force him to say that baqā' of baqā' is also additional.

Guided Path
1
Identify the response
Find the sentence: 'the answer that baqā' of baqā' is the same as baqā''. Understand what it tries to solve (infinite regress of baqā's).
2
Examine the first objection
The first objection: if baqā' of baqā' is identical, then God's own baqā' could also be identical to Himself, making it problematic to prove that baqā' does not need a further baqā'.
This challenges the uniqueness of the answer.
3
Examine the second objection
The second objection: the opponent (who says baqā' is additional) cannot accept this answer because his own proof for baqā' being additional would also prove that baqā' of baqā' is additional, leading to regress.
The denier's argument is conclusive as an objection, even if not as a proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الباري تعالى أيضاً نفسه وإثبات أن البقاء لا يحتاج في البقاء إلى بقاء زائد بخلاف غيره مشكل، وأما ثانياً فلأن الخصم القائل بكون البقاء صفة وجودية زائدة على الوجود لا يمكنه هذا الجواب ولا القول بجواز أن The second: if baqā', on the assumption of its being existent, needs the essence, then circularity is necessary, because the essence also needs baqā', since its existence in the second moment is caused by it. Otherwise, the essence would be needful of it while it (baqā') would be independent. Commentary
This passage presents the second proof against baqā' being an additional attribute and then engages in a lengthy critical analysis of the responses. The second proof is an argument from circularity: if baqā' (on the assumption that it is an existent attribute) needs the essence (i.e., depends on the divine essence for its existence), then there is circularity because the essence itself needs baqā' for its continued existence in the second moment. Misconception & Correction

Why can the opponent (who says baqā' is additional) not accept the response that baqā' of baqā' is identical?

C) Because he does not believe in baqā' at all.
D) Because it is too complex.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The identity response was meant to stop a regress but created new problems. Reflect on a time you used a shortcut in your thinking about God to avoid a difficult question. Did it truly resolve the issue, or did it only mask it? What would it cost you to face the question honestly?
?
This passage shows that even well-intentioned theological reasoning can fail and yet still serve as an effective objection. Are you willing to hold your own beliefs to the same standard—accepting that your defenses might be mere ilzām rather than conclusive proof? How does that humility change your relationship with truth?

The Principle of Repetition in Kind and Its Refutation
Examination of the principle of repetition in kind and its interaction with the response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate surrounding the principle 'whatever repeats in kind must be mentally-posited' and how it relates to the refutation of the opponent's claim.

Core Idea
The principle that repetition in kind implies mental positing is contested, and refuting a proof does not refute the claim itself.

The discussion now turns to a principle from al-Talwīḥāt: if something repeats in kind, it must be mentally-posited to avoid infinite regress. The commentator explores whether this principle refutes the previous response or is itself refuted by it, and clarifies that refuting a proof does not necessarily invalidate the claim.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle
The principle: 'whatever repeats in kind must be mentally-posited'. If baqā' of baqā' were not mentally-posited, infinite regress would result.
This principle is used to argue that baqā' itself might be mentally-posited.
2
Analyze the relation between principle and response
The passage discusses whether the principle refutes the response 'baqā' of baqā' identical' or vice versa. It suggests a possible reading where 'yuriddu' is passive, making the principle a repetition used to answer a hypothetical question.
This shows the complexity of the argument.
3
Grasp the final distinction
The commentator notes that refuting the proof does not refute the claim; it only removes the evidence. Unless 'refutation' means 'lack of proof' rather than 'falsity'.
This is a key methodological point in dialectic.

الباري تعالى أيضاً نفسه وإثبات أن البقاء لا يحتاج في البقاء إلى بقاء زائد بخلاف غيره مشكل، وأما ثانياً فلأن الخصم القائل بكون البقاء صفة وجودية زائدة على الوجود لا يمكنه هذا الجواب ولا القول بجواز أن يكون بقاء البقاء مثلاً اعتبارياً كما سبق في نظائره، وذلك لأن دليله الذي استدل به على كون البقاء صفة وجودية زائدة على الوجود يدل على كون بقاء البقاء صفة وجودية زائدة على البقاء إذ البقاء يتحقق بدونه كما في ثاني زمان الحدوث، ويتجدد بعده صفة بقاء البقاء، والحاصل أن دليل النافي يتم إلزاماً وإن لم يتم برهاناً. قوله: (ويرد على هذا الجواب إلخ) فيه بحث أشرنا إليه في الأمور العامة وهو أن هذا الجواب يرد على قولهم: ما تكرر نوعه إلخ لأن هذا القول يرد على ذلك الجواب، وتوضيحه أن هذا القول ضابطة ذكرها صاحب التلويحات وبينها بأنها لو لم يكن اعتبارياً لزم التسلسل في الأمور الموجودة المترتبة، وإذا منع لزوم التسلسل يكون بقاء البقاء مثلاً عين البقاء لا فكيف يثبت بها ما هو المطلوب أعني كون البقاء أمراً اعتبارياً، نعم لو ثبت اعتراف المثبت بوجودية البقاء بتلك القاعدة لأمكن توجيه الإيراد بأن خلاصته هو أن ادعاءهم وجودية البقاء وانقطاع التسلسل بكون بقائه عينه يرده اعترافهم بأن ما تكرر نوعه يجب أن يكون اعتبارياً سواء تم دليله أم لا، ويمكن أن يجعل لفظ يرد على صيغة المجهول من الرد لا على صيغة المعلوم من الورود ويجعل أن ما تكرر نوعه إلخ فاعله فحينئذ يكون إعادة لما ذكره في الأمور العامة ويكون فائدة الإعادة دفع سؤال متوهم وهو أنه كيف يدعي وجودية البقاء وكون بقائه عينه أنه قد سابق أن ما تكرر نوعه يجب كونه اعتبارياً، فأشار إلى دفعه بأن تلك القاعدة مردودة بهذا الجواب، وعلى هذا أيضاً يندفع البحث لكن يرد عليه بأن هذا الجواب يرد دليل ذلك المدعى ورد الدليل لا يكون رداً للمدعي لأن إبطال الملزوم لا يفيد إبطال اللازم، سيما والمستفاد من الدليل المذكور العلم بالمدلول فاللازم من إبطاله انتفاء ذلك العلم لا انتفاء نفس المدلول، اللهم إلا أن يريد بالرد مجرد عدم ثبوته لا بطلانه في نفسه فإن ذلك القدر يكفي هاهنا لدفع ذلك السؤال فليتأمل. قوله: (لزم الدور) لا يقال: احتياج البقاء إلى الذات بالنسبة إلى وجودها مطلقاً وعكسه
The second: if baqā', on the assumption of its being existent, needs the essence, then circularity is necessary, because the essence also needs baqā', since its existence in the second moment is caused by it. Otherwise, the essence would be needful of it while it (baqā') would be independent. I say: if this is granted, it should be restricted to the fact that the renewal of baqā' entails that the Exalted is a locus of accidents; and it may be said that this is a matter of specifying the way. His statement: (And the answer that baqā' of baqā' is the same as baqā') is debatable. First, because then it would be permissible that the baqā' of the Exalted Himself is also the same, and proving that baqā' does not need a further baqā' unlike others is problematic. Second, because the opponent who holds baqā' to be an additional existent attribute cannot accept this answer nor the claim that baqā' of baqā' is mentally-posited, as in similar cases, because his proof for baqā' being additional also proves that baqā' of baqā' is additional, since baqā' is realized without it as in the second moment of origination, and afterwards the attribute of baqā' of baqā' is renewed. The upshot is that the denier's argument is conclusive as an objection even if not as a proof. His statement: (And it is objected to this answer etc.) has a discussion we referred to in the General Matters, namely that this answer refutes their statement 'whatever repeats in kind etc.' because that statement refutes the answer. The explanation is that this statement is a principle mentioned by the author of al-Talwīḥāt, and he explained that if it were not mentally-posited, there would be infinite regress in successive existent things. If infinite regress is prevented, then baqā' of baqā' would be identical to baqā', so how can the desired point — that baqā' is mentally-posited — be established by it? Yes, if the affirmant's admission of baqā' being existent by that principle were established, the objection could be directed such that its gist is: their claim of baqā' being existent and the cessation of regress by identifying it with baqā' itself is refuted by their admission that whatever repeats in kind must be mentally-posited, whether its proof is sound or not. And the word 'yuriddu' (is refuted) can be made passive of 'radda' rather than active of 'warada', and 'that which repeats in kind etc.' can be made its subject; then it would be a repetition of what was mentioned in the General Matters, and the benefit of repetition is to repel a hypothetical question: how can he claim existence of baqā' and that its baqā' is identical to it when it was previously established that whatever repeats in kind must be mentally-posited? So he indicated its repulsion by saying that that principle is refuted by this answer. And with this, the investigation is also repelled, but it is objected that this answer refutes the proof of that claim, and refuting the proof is not a refutation of the claimer, because invalidating the necessary does not invalidate the necessary consequence, especially since what is derived from that proof is knowledge of the conclusion; the necessary consequence of its invalidation is the absence of that knowledge, not the absence of the conclusion itself, unless by 'refutation' he means mere lack of proof, not its falsity in itself, for that much suffices here to repel the question. So let it be considered. His statement: (Circularity is necessary) cannot be said: the need of baqā' for the essence is with respect to its existence absolutely, and vice versa.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof against baqā' being an additional attribute and then engages in a lengthy critical analysis of the responses. The second proof is an argument from circularity: if baqā' (on the assumption that it is an existent attribute) needs the essence (i.e., depends on the divine essence for its existence), then there is circularity because the essence itself needs baqā' for its continued existence in the second moment. If, on the other hand, baqā' does not need the essence, then the essence would be needful of baqā' while baqā' is independent, which is also problematic. The commentator then interjects a remark that if this reasoning is granted, it should be limited to the point that the renewal of baqā' would imply that the Exalted is a locus of accidents — a different issue. Next, the discussion turns to the response that 'baqā' of baqā' is the same as baqā''. This response is criticized on two fronts: first, it would allow that God's own baqā' is identical to Himself, which is problematic; second, the opponent who holds baqā' to be an additional attribute cannot use this response because his own reasoning would force him to say that baqā' of baqā' is also additional, leading to an infinite regress. The passage then delves into the principle 'whatever repeats in kind must be mentally-posited', attributed to the author of al-Talwīḥāt. There is a debate about whether this principle refutes the response or is itself refuted by it. The commentator suggests that the word 'yuriddu' can be read as passive, turning the principle into a repetition from an earlier discussion, used to answer a hypothetical question. However, a further objection arises: refuting the proof does not refute the claim itself unless 'refutation' is understood as merely showing the lack of proof. The passage ends with a final note on the circularity argument, stating that one cannot claim that baqā' needs the essence in an absolute sense while vice versa also holds — presumably an incomplete thought. Overall, this is a dense dialectical passage exploring multiple layers of objection and response.

Key Terms
تسلسل
اعتباري
تلويحات
إلزام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that refuting an opponent's proof automatically disproves their claim.
Correction: The passage explains that refuting a proof only removes the evidence, unless 'refutation' means 'lack of proof'.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does refuting a proof do?
A) It automatically disproves the claim.
B) It removes the evidence for the claim, but not necessarily the claim itself.
C) It proves the claim is false.
D) It has no effect on the claim.

Key Takeaway
Refuting an opponent's proof does not automatically refute their claim; careful distinction is needed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When someone attacks your reasoning for a deeply held belief, do you immediately feel the need to abandon the belief, or do you distinguish between the weakness of your argument and the truth of the belief itself? What does this lesson call you to do differently?
?
Think of a time you dismissed a truth because the person presenting it had a flawed argument. How does this lesson expose a lack of intellectual honesty in yourself? Will you commit to evaluating claims independently of the strength of the arguments you first encounter?
?
If refuting a proof does not refute the claim, then your own faith rests not on any single argument but on something deeper. Have you ever mistaken a compelling argument for the foundation of your faith? What would it mean for you to seek the truth beyond arguments?

Baqa' and Infinite Regress: Argument and Response
Presents the argument that baqa' would cause infinite regress and the response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that baqa' leads to infinite regress and the response that baqa' of baqa' is identical to baqa'.

Core Idea
Baqa' of baqa' is baqa' itself, halting the regress.

This lesson examines a key argument in Kalam about the attribute of baqa' (permanence). The argument claims that if baqa' were contingent, it would require an infinite series of baqa'ats. The response, drawing an analogy to existence, asserts that baqa' of baqa' is the same as baqa'. However, an objection is raised that repeated types must be accidental. The commentator clarifies that this objection is refuted by earlier principles.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Note the key terms: بقاء, تسلسل, اعتباري.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Trace the argument
The argument starts: if baqa' were to cease, then sequential baqa'ats would form an infinite regress. Understand why this is problematic.
This is a logical regress, not temporal.
3
Analyze the response
The response denies the regress by stating that the baqa' of baqa' is identical to baqa', analogous to existence of existence. This halts the regress because no new attribute is added.
This is a key move in Kalam to avoid infinite regress of attributes.
4
Consider the objection and commentary
The objection claims that anything repeated in type must be accidental (iʿtibārī). The commentator notes that this very objection is refuted by earlier discussions in al-Umūr al-ʿĀmmah.
This shows the interconnectedness of Kalam arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البقاء المفروض زواله وحينئذ تتسلسل البقاءات المترتبة الموجودة معاً، والجواب : إن بقاء البقاء نفس البقاء كما قيل في وجود الوجود، ووجوب الوجوب، وإمكان الإمكان فلا تسلسل أصلا ويرد على هذا الجواب أن ما تكرر نوعه يجب كونه اعتبارياً كما مر. قوله : ( ويرد على هذا الجواب إلخ ) فيه بحث أشرنا إليه في الأمور العامة وهو أن هذا الجواب يرد على قولهم : ما تكرر نوعه إلخ لأن هذا القول يرد على ذلك الجواب.
If baqa' were to cease, then the sequential baqa'ats existing together would form an infinite regress. The answer: the baqa' of baqa' is the same as baqa', as said in existence of existence, necessity of necessity, and possibility of possibility, so no infinite regress. This answer is objected to [by saying] that what is repeated in type must be accidental (iʿtibārī) as previously mentioned. His statement: 'And there is a response to this answer etc.' contains a discussion we referred to in al-Umūr al-ʿĀmmah, which is that this answer refutes their statement: 'what is repeated in type etc.' because this statement refutes that answer.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument and its response. The argument posits that if baqa' (permanence) were to cease (i.e., if it were contingent), then an infinite regress of sequential baqa'ats would occur simultaneously. The response draws an analogy to existence, necessity, and possibility: just as the existence of existence is existence itself, so the baqa' of baqa' is baqa' itself, thus halting the regress. An objection is then raised: anything whose type is repeated must be accidental (iʿtibārī), not real. The commentator (al-Jurjani) notes that this objection is answered by the earlier discussion in al-Umūr al-ʿĀmmah, where the same principle is used to refute the objection itself.

Key Terms
بقاء
بقاء البقاء
تسلسل
اعتباري
وجود الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The infinite regress in the argument about baqa' is a temporal regress (one after another in time).
Correction: The regress is logical and simultaneous: all the baqa'ats would exist at once, making it absurd.

Checkpoint
How does the response avoid infinite regress in the attribute of baqa'?
A) By denying the existence of baqa' altogether
B) By asserting that baqa' of baqa' is identical to baqa'
C) By claiming that baqa' is not a real attribute
D) By arguing that infinite regress is possible for God

Key Takeaway
The baqa' of baqa' is identical to baqa', preventing infinite regress in divine attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly rely on God's permanence (baqa') as the foundation of your own existence, or do you implicitly trust in things that will cease? What would change if you internalized that only His baqa' is real?
?
When you face objections to your faith that seem logically strong, do you dismiss them or engage deeply like the scholars? What step can you take this week to understand one such objection thoroughly?
?
The concept of 'baqa' of baqa'' shows that the ultimate reality is self-referential and non-regressive. Is your relationship with God anchored in that same self-evident certainty, or is it based on secondary things that need further justification? What needs to change?

Second Argument on Baqa': Circularity and Response
Presents the circularity argument and response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the circularity objection against baqa' being an existential attribute and the response that denies real dependence between the essence and baqa'.

Core Idea
Circularity is avoided by denying that baqa' causes existence in the second moment.

This passage presents a key objection to the view that baqa' is an existential attribute: it would create a circular dependence between the essence and baqa'. The response clarifies that existence in the second moment merely coincides with baqa', not caused by it. Understanding this distinction is crucial for proper kalām reasoning about divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read and identify the two alternatives
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice that the argument presents two possible scenarios: either baqa' depends on the essence (leading to circularity) or it does not (leading to baqa' becoming the necessary existent).
The argument uses conditional reasoning (law… la…) to show both options are problematic.
2
Analyze the response
The response denies that the essence depends on baqa'. It rejects the claim that existence in the second moment is caused by baqa'. Instead, it says existence merely occurs concurrently (bimusāḥaba) with baqa'.
The key term is 'ittiḥād al-zamān' (concomitance in time), not causation.
3
Understand the concept of concommitance
The response introduces the idea that two things can exist together without one being the cause of the other. This is a crucial nuance in kalām to avoid unwanted metaphysical commitments.
Compare with the notion of 'ittiḥād' often used in discussions of attributes.
4
Reflect on theological significance
This argument and response protect the uniqueness of Allah as the necessary being. If baqa' were independent of the essence, it would rival Allah's necessity. The response preserves tawḥīd by denying real causality between attributes.
This connects to broader discussions of divine simplicity and attribute ontology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: لو احتاج البقاء على تقدير كونه وجودياً إلى الذات لزم الدور، لأن الذات محتاجة إلى البقاء أيضاً، فإن وجوده في الزمان الثاني معلل به. وإلا، أي وإن لم يحتج البقاء إلى الذات، لكان الذات محتاجاً إليه وكان مستغنياً عن الذات مع استغنائه عن غيره أيضاً، فكان البقاء هو الواجب الوجود دون الذات. والجواب: منع احتياج الذات إليه. وما قيل من أن وجوده في الزمن الثاني معلل به ممنوع، غاية ما في الباب أن وجوده فيه لا يكون إلا مع البقاء، وذلك لا يوجب أن يكون البقاء علة لوجوده فيه إذ يجوز أن يكون تحققهما على سبيل الاتفاق. وإليه الإشارة بقوله: وإن اتفق تحققهما معاً.
Second: If baqa', assuming it is existential, depends on the essence, then circularity follows, because the essence also depends on baqa', since its existence in the second moment is caused by it. Otherwise, meaning if baqa' does not depend on the essence, then the essence would be in need of it and it would be independent of the essence and also independent of other than it, so baqa' would be the necessary existence instead of the essence. The answer: the claim that the essence depends on it is denied. And what is said that its existence in the second moment is caused by it is denied; the utmost is that its existence in that moment does not occur except with baqa', and that does not necessitate that baqa' is the cause of its existence therein, since it is possible that the two occur concomitantly. This is indicated by his statement: 'even if their occurrence is together.'
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument and its response. The argument states that if baqa' (as an existential attribute) depends on the essence (dhāt), then circularity (dawr) arises because the essence also depends on baqa' (since its existence in the second moment is caused by baqa'). Alternatively, if baqa' does not depend on the essence, then the essence depends on baqa' while baqa' is independent of the essence and everything else, making baqa' the necessary being, not the essence. The response denies that the essence depends on baqa'. It further rejects that existence in the second moment is caused by baqa'; rather, existence merely occurs concurrently with baqa' (ittiḥād al-zamān). The commentator notes that this refutes the objection.

Key Terms
دور
احتياج الذات
علة
اتفاق
واجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The circularity objection is between two independent entities, making it impossible to resolve.
Correction: The circularity is between the essence and an attribute; the response shows that the dependence is not real but merely temporal concommitance.

Checkpoint
What does the response claim about the relationship between existence in the second moment and baqa'?
A) Baqa' is the cause of existence in the second moment
B) Existence in the second moment is identical to baqa'
C) Existence in the second moment occurs only with baqa' but not necessarily caused by it
D) Baqa' is not needed for existence in the second moment

Key Takeaway
The response to the circularity objection shows that existence and baqa' can be concomitant without causation, preserving the uniqueness of the necessary being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you ever act as if some creature or circumstance is independent of God, even while believing He is the only self-sufficient? Where specifically might you be committing the second horn of that dilemma – attributing independence to something other than Allah?
?
When you see your own continued existence (baqa') from one moment to the next, do you implicitly assume it comes from yourself? How does the truth that your existence is merely concomitant with Allah's will, not caused by any inherent attribute of yours, reduce your pride?
?
This passage denies that baqa' causes existence – even for God. Are you comfortable with the idea that no attribute of God causes His existence, but only accompanies it? What does that say about the utter simplicity and self-sufficiency of Allah – and what can you rely on other than Him?
