Majority Interpretation of Istiwa' as Overpowering
Sanusi presents the majority interpretation of istiwa' as overpowering with poetic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the majority Ash'ari interpretation of istiwa' in the Throne verse as overpowering, supported by classical Arabic poetry, and how it returns to the attribute of divine power.

Core Idea
Istiwa' over the Throne means overpowering, not physical sitting.

The Qur'anic verse 'The Most Merciful is established over the Throne' has been interpreted by the majority of Ash'ari scholars as a metaphor for domination. This lesson examines their reasoning and the poetic evidence they cited to support this linguistic usage, establishing that the attribute of istiwa' ultimately refers to divine power (qudra).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the English translation. Notice the structure: Sanusi presents the attribute, the difference of opinion, and the majority view with poetic evidence.
The poets' lines serve as evidence for the linguistic meaning of istīlā'.
2
Identify the majority interpretation
The passage states that the majority said istiwa' means istīlā' (overpowering) and that it returns to the attribute of power. Why does this matter? It avoids attributing spatial location to Allah.
3
Understand the poetic evidence
Look at the two lines of poetry. The first uses istawā 'alā to mean 'overpowered' in a political context. The second uses the verb in a military context. How do these support the linguistic claim that istiwa' can mean domination?
Classical Arabic poetry was often used as evidence for word meanings.
4
Connect to the attribute of power
The passage says that if istiwa' means istīlā', then it ultimately refers to the attribute of power (qudra). Reflect on how this aligns with the principle that Allah's attributes are not like created things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصفة الثالثة : الاستواء لما وصف تعالى بالاستواء في قوله تعالى : {الرحمن على العرش استوى} [طه: ٥]، اختلف الأصحاب فيه فقال الأكثرون : هو الاستيلاء ويعود الاستواء حينئذ إلى صفة القدرة قال الشاعر : قد استوى عمرو على العراق من غير سيف ودم مهراق أي استولى وقال الآخر: فلما علونا واستوينا عليهم تركناهم صرعى لنسر وطائر أي استولينا لا يقال : الاستواء بمعنى الاستيلاء يشعر بالاضطراب والمقاومة والمغالبة، أي يشعر بسبق هذه الأمور التي تستحيل في حقه تعالى وأيضاً فلا فائدة لتخصيص العرش لأن استيلاءه يعم الكل لأنا نجيب عن الأول بمنع الإشعار ألا ترى أن الغالب لا يشعر به كما في قوله تعالى : {والله غالب على أمره} [يوسف: ٢١]، نعم ربما يفهم سبق تلك الأمور من خصوصية من أسند إليه الاستيلاء في أمر مخصوص وعن الثاني بأن الفائدة هي الإشعار بالأعلى على الأذنى إذ مقرر في الأوهام أن العرش أعظم الخلق فإذا استولى عليه كان مستوليا على قطعاً، وهذا عكس ما هو المشهور من التنبيه بالأدنى على الأعلى وكلاهما صواب، فإنه كما يفهم من حكم الأدنى حكم الأعلى إذا كان به أولى . كذلك يفهم عكسه إذا كان الأدنى بالحكم أولى ، وقيل : هو أي الاستواء هاهنا القصد فيعود إلى صفة الإرادة ونحو قوله تعالى : {ثم استوى إلى السماء} [البقرة: ٢٩]، أي قصد إليها وهو بعيد إذ ذلك تعدى بإلى كالقصد دون على كاستيلاء وذهب غيره. قوله : ( إذ مقرر في الأوهام إلخ) دفع لما يقال : لعل الله تعالى مخلوقاً أعظم . قوله : (إذ ذلك تعدى بإلى والتضمين خلاف الأصل فلا يصار إليه بلا ضرورة .
The third attribute: istiwā' (being established). Since the Exalted is described with istiwā' in His statement: 'The Most Merciful is established over the Throne' (Q 20:5), the companions differed concerning it. The majority said: it is al-istīlā' (overpowering), and then istiwā' returns to the attribute of power (al-qudra). The poet said: 'Amr has istawā over Iraq without sword or shed blood' meaning he overpowered. And another said: 'When we rose and istawaynā over them, we left them corpses for vultures and birds' meaning we overpowered. It should not be said that istiwā' in the sense of istīlā' suggests agitation, resistance, and overcoming, meaning it suggests the precedence of these things which are impossible with respect to Him, and also there is no benefit in specifying the Throne because His overpowering encompasses everything. For we answer the first by denying the suggestion; do you not see that the overpowerer is not suggested? As in His statement: 'And Allah is overpowering over His affair' (Q 12:21). Yes, perhaps the precedence of those things is understood from the specificity of the one to whom overpowering is attributed in a specific matter. And concerning the second, the benefit is the indication of the higher over the lower, since it is established in the minds that the Throne is the greatest of creation, so if He overpowered it, He is overpowering over everything without doubt. This is the opposite of the well-known method of drawing attention from the lower to the higher, and both are correct, because just as the ruling of the lower is understood for the higher if it is more appropriate, likewise its opposite is understood if the lower is more appropriate in ruling. And it was said: istiwā' here is al-qasd (intention), and then it returns to the attribute of will (al-irāda), similar to His statement: 'Then He istawā to the heaven' (Q 2:29), meaning He intended it. This is far-fetched because that verb is transitive with 'ilā' like qasd, not with 'alā' like istīlā'. And others went differently. His saying: 'since it is established in the minds etc.' is a repudiation of what might be said: perhaps Allah, exalted, created something greater. His saying: 'since that is transitive with 'ilā' and the inclusion (al-tadmīn) is contrary to the principle, so it is not resorted to without necessity.'
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage discusses the divine attribute of istiwā' (establishment/being seated) in light of the verse 'The Most Merciful is established over the Throne.' It presents the majority interpretation as istīlā' (overpowering/domination), which reduces istiwā' to the attribute of power (qudra). It cites poetic evidence for this linguistic usage. The passage then refutes two objections: (1) that istīlā' implies struggle, which is impossible for God; (2) that specifying the Throne is pointless since God's power is universal. The first objection is answered by denying that istīlā' necessarily implies struggle, citing the Quranic phrase 'Allah is overpowering over His affair.' The second objection is answered by noting the educational benefit: mentioning the Throne (the greatest creation) emphasizes that God's domination includes everything, following a principle of drawing attention from the higher to the lower. The passage also mentions a minority interpretation: istiwā' as qasd (intention), which reduces it to the attribute of will (irāda), but dismisses it as far-fetched due to grammatical considerations (istawā is used with 'ilā' when meaning intention, but with 'alā' here). The commentary then clarifies the two 'qāla' notes: one rebuts the possibility of a greater creature, and the other rejects the interpretation of istiwā' as intention because it requires assuming a different verb form (tadmīn).

Key Terms
استواء (istiwā')
استيلاء (istīlā')
قدرة (qudra)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What do the majority of Ash'ari scholars say istiwa' in the Throne verse means?
A) Physical sitting
B) Overpowering (istīlā')
C) Intention (qasd)
D) Mercy

Key Takeaway
The majority of Ash'ari scholars interpret istiwa' over the Throne as divine overpowering, supported by classical Arabic usage, thereby affirming Allah's absolute power without spatial placement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'ar-Rahmanu 'ala al-'arshi istawa', does your mind immediately conjure a physical throne and a seated figure? How will you actively train your heart to think of Allah's absolute power instead? What specific reminder will you place in your daily life to reinforce this tanzih?
?
The majority of scholars set aside the apparent meaning and used pre-Islamic poetry to interpret this verse. Are you willing to submit your own understanding to the consensus of qualified scholars, even when it feels counter-intuitive? Which belief do you hold that might need a similar reinterpretation based on sound scholarship?
?
If istiwa' is really about overpowering (istila'), then Allah controls everything completely. How does this truth expose your own illusions of control? When will you next surrender a specific anxiety or plan to His overpowering will, and what concrete action will accompany that surrender?

Refuting Objections and the Minority Interpretation of Istiwa'
Sanusi refutes two objections against istīlā' and rejects the qasd interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari scholars refute objections to interpreting istiwa' as overpowering and why the interpretation of istiwa' as intention is rejected on linguistic grounds.

Core Idea
Objections to istīlā' are answered, and istiwa' as intention is grammatically untenable.

The interpretation of istiwa' as overpowering (istīlā') faces two main objections: that it implies struggle, and that specifying the Throne is unnecessary. This lesson presents the Ash'ari responses to these objections, as well as a minority interpretation that istiwa' means intention, which is dismissed on linguistic grounds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic and English. Note that the passage begins with 'It should not be said...' which introduces the objections.
The passage also includes two marginal comments from the commentator, marked by 'qāla'.
2
Analyze the first objection and its answer
The first objection claims istīlā' suggests agitation. The answer denies this, citing the Quranic phrase 'Allah is overpowering over His affair.' Consider why this is a strong refutation.
The objection misunderstands the nature of divine overpowering as temporal.
3
Analyze the second objection and its answer
The second objection questions why the Throne is specified if God's power is universal. The answer explains the pedagogical benefit: mentioning the greatest creation implies domination over all.
This uses the principle of indicating the higher over the lower.
4
Examine the minority interpretation and its rebuttal
Some said istiwa' means intention (qasd), but it is dismissed because the verb 'istawā' with 'alā' means overpowering, not intention. The grammar rule (tadmīn) is rejected as unnecessary.

Qidam as a Negative Attribute: Meaning and Defense
Al-Jurjani defines qidam as negative and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the attribute of qidam (eternity) is classified as a negative attribute in kalam and how Al-Jurjani defends this view against an objection.

Core Idea
Qidam is not a positive attribute; it means Allah's existence is not in space.

In this passage, Al-Jurjani clarifies that qidam (eternity) is a negative attribute, not a positive one. He then addresses a potential objection that the negation (non-spatiality) is caused by eternity itself. This lesson unpacks the logic behind this classification and its significance in the broader kalam tradition.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the objection and response structure.
The Arabic includes parenthetical glosses from the commentator.
2
Identify the main claim
Find where Al-Jurjani states that qidam is a negative attribute and what it negates. He says 'its reference is existence not in space'.
3
Understand the objection and response
The objection claims that the negative (non-spatiality) is caused by eternity itself. The response is that negative attributes are not caused by positive things— the negative is the very meaning of eternity.
Notice the distinction between negative and positive attributes in causality.
4
Reflect on the broader conclusion
Al-Jurjani states that qidam is a mental consideration (i'tibari) with no external existence, which refutes the opponent's position. Think about why this matters for the overall argument.
This connects to earlier discussions on general matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الثامن : في أنه تعالى صفة القدم أمراً سلبياً إذ مرجعه حينئذ إلى وجوده لا في حيز ، فإن قلت : هذا السلبي معلل بالقدم لا نفسه قلت : إن الصفات السلبية لا تعلل بخلاف الثبوتية ( أو غيرهما ) من المعاني ( فالتصوير) أي فعليه تصوير ذلك المعنى المراد أولا ) ثم التقرير) والتحقيق بإقامة الدليل عليه ثانياً (هذا) الذي أوردناه هاهنا في إبطاله ) منضم إلى ما سبق ) في مباحث الأمور العامة ( من أنه ( أي القدم أمر ( اعتباري ) لا وجود له في الخارج، فإنه يدل على بطلان مذهبه ودليله أيضاً . قوله : (إذ مرجعه حينئذ إلى وجوده لا في حيز في العبارة مساهلة والمقصود أن مرجعه انتفاء التحيز فلا يرد أن الوجود ليس بسلبي . قوله : قلت : إن الصفات السلبية لا تعلل أي لا تعلل بالوجودي فيكون هذا السلبي أو سلبي آخر نفس القدم لا معلوله فلا يرد تعليل عدم التحيز بالتجرد والحاجة بالإمكان .
The fourth observation - the eighth intention: On that He, exalted, is the attribute of eternity as a negative matter, because its reference then is to His existence not in space. If you say: This negative is caused by eternity not itself, I say: Negative attributes are not caused, unlike positive ones (or others) of meanings. (So the depiction) i.e., the depiction of that intended meaning first, (then the establishment) and verification by presenting the proof upon it second. (This) which we have presented here in its refutation (is joined to what preceded) in the discussions of general matters (that) eternity is a (mental consideration) having no existence in the external, for it indicates the invalidity of his doctrine and his proof also. His statement: (His reference then to His existence not in space) in the expression is a looseness, and the intended is that its reference is the negation of spatial location, so it is not objected that existence is not negative. His statement: I said: Negative attributes are not caused, i.e., they are not caused by a positive thing, so this negative or another negative is the very eternity, not its effect, so it is not objected that the negation of spatial location is caused by being abstract and the need for possibility.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage defines the attribute of eternity (qidam) as a negative attribute. The author explains that qidam means that God's existence is not in a spatial location (hayyiz). An objection is raised: the negative (non-spatiality) is caused by eternity itself, not the other way around. The answer is that negative attributes are not caused by positive attributes; rather, the negative (non-spatiality) is itself the meaning of eternity, not its effect. The author then notes that this discussion is part of a broader refutation, concluding that eternity is a mental consideration (i'tibari) without external existence, which invalidates the opponent's position. The marginal gloss clarifies that the phrase 'reference to His existence not in space' is a loose expression; the precise meaning is the negation of spatial location, and that negative attributes are not caused by positive things, so the argument stands.

Key Terms
قدم (qidam)
صفة سلبية (sifah salbiyyah)
وجود (wujud)
حيز (hayyiz)
اعتباري (i'tibari)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that qidam is a positive attribute indicating timeless existence.
Correction: Qidam is negative: it only negates spatial location from Allah, not a positive quality.

Checkpoint
What is the meaning of the attribute of qidam according to this passage?
A) Positive attribute of eternal existence in time
B) Negative attribute of non-existence in space
C) Attribute of power
D) Attribute of will

Key Takeaway
Qidam is a negative attribute meaning that Allah's existence is not confined to space; it is a mental consideration, not a positive reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah is not located in any space, then He is not 'far' or 'near' in a physical sense. When you feel distant from Allah, where does that feeling come from? How can you train your heart to seek closeness to a Being who is not defined by place?
?
Negative attributes like 'Allah is not limited by space' or 'Allah is not in time' protect His majesty, but do you ever feel that they make Him seem impersonal or remote? What would you need to let go of in your imagination to embrace this transcendence without losing intimacy?
?
Al-Jurjani says qidam is a 'mental consideration' — it exists only in the mind, not outside. But you still affirm it as true. In what areas of your own life have you settled for a lesser, 'physical' understanding of Allah because the deeper reality feels too abstract? What is one step you will take today to worship Allah as He truly is, beyond your imagination?

The Majority Interpretation of Istiwā': Dominion (Istīlā')
Al-Jurjani presents the majority interpretation of istiwā' as istīlā' and defends it against objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the majority position that istiwā' means dominion, how it is supported by poetry and logic, and how the objections are answered.

Core Idea
Istiwā' means dominion, reducing to God's power, supported by poetry and reason.

The attribute of istiwā' (establishing over the Throne) is a pivotal Qur'anic description of Allah. The majority of scholars interpret it as istīlā' (dominion), thereby returning it to the attribute of power. In this lesson, we examine their evidence and how they respond to two key objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the majority view
Read the Arabic passage that presents the majority interpretation of istiwā' as istīlā', including the poetic evidence. Then read the translation.
Notice how the examples from poetry illustrate the use of istiwā' meaning domination.
2
Identify the two objections
The passage mentions two objections to the majority interpretation. Find them and understand why they are raised.
The first objection concerns the implications of istīlā'; the second concerns the specification of the Throne.
3
Examine the responses to each objection
Read the answers provided. The first response denies that istīlā' necessarily implies struggle; the second response explains the benefit of mentioning the Throne.
The argument from the greater is a key logical tool here.
4
Reflect on the argument from the greater
Consider how the Throne being the greatest creation implies that domination over it means domination over all. This is an a fortiori argument.
This argument counters the objection that specifying the Throne is pointless.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصفة ( الثالثة : الاستواء لما وصف تعالى بالاستواء في قوله تعالى : الرحمن على العرش استوى ﴾ [ طه : ٥]، اختلف الأصحاب فيه فقال الأكثرون : هو الاستيلاء ويعود الاستواء حينئذ ) إلى ( صفة ( القدرة ) قال الشاعر : ) قد استوى عمرو على العراق من غير سيف ودم مهراق أي استولى وقال الآخر: فلما علونا واستوينا عليهم تركناهم صرعى لنسر وطائر أي استولينا لا يقال : الاستواء بمعنى الاستيلاء ) يشعر بالاضطراب والمقاومة والمغالبة)، أي يشعر بسبق هذه الأمور التي تستحيل في حقه تعالى ( وأيضاً فلا فائدة لتخصيص العرش لأن استيلاءه يعم الكل ( لأنا) نجيب عن الأول بمنع الإشعار ألا ترى أن الغالب لا يشعر به كما في قوله تعالى : والله غالب على أمره ﴾ [يوسف : ۲۱] ، نعم ربما يفهم سبق تلك الأمور من خصوصية من أسند إليه الاستيلاء في أمر مخصوص ( وعن الثاني بأن الفائدة ( هي ( الإشعار بالأعلى على الأذنى إذ مقرر في الأوهام أن العرش أعظم الخلق ) فإذا استولى عليه كان مستوليا على قطعاً، وهذا عكس ما هو المشهور من التنبيه بالأدنى على الأعلى وكلاهما صواب، فإنه كما يفهم من حكم الأدنى حكم الأعلى إذا كان به أولى . كذلك يفهم عكسه إذا كان الأدنى بالحكم أولى ، وقيل : هو ( أي الاستواء هاهنا ( القصد ) فيعود ) إلى صفة الإرادة ( ونحو) قوله تعالى : ثم استوى إلى السماء ﴾ [البقرة : ٢٩ ]، أي قصد إليها ( وهو بعيد إذ ذلك تعدى بإلى كالقصد ( دون على ) كاستيلاء قوله : ( إذ مقرر في الأوهام إلخ) دفع لما يقال : لعل الله تعالى مخلوقاً أعظم . قوله : (إذ ذلك تعدى بإلى والتضمين خلاف الأصل فلا يصار إليه بلا ضرورة . وذهب الشيخ في أحد قوليه إلى أنه ( أي الاستواء (صفة زائدة ) ليست عائدة إلى الصفات السابقة وإن لم نعلمها بعينها ، ( ولم يقم دليل عليه ولا يجوز التعويل) في إثباته ( على الظواهر من الآيات والأحاديث ( مع قيام الاحتمال ) المذكور وهو أن يراد به ) الاستيلاء أو القصد على ضعف، فالحق التوقف مع القطع بأنه ليس كاستواء الأجسام .
The third attribute: al-istiwā'. When He, exalted, described Himself with istiwā' in His saying: 'The Most Merciful upon the Throne established' [Taha:5], the companions differed about it. The majority said: it is al-istīlā' (dominion), and istiwā' then returns to the attribute of power. The poet said: 'Amr established over Iraq without sword or shed blood' i.e., he dominated. And another said: 'When we rose and established over them, we left them prostrate for vulture and bird' i.e., we dominated. It is not said: that istiwā' meaning istīlā' suggests disturbance, resistance, and overcoming, i.e., it suggests the precedence of these matters which are impossible in His right. And also there is no benefit in specifying the Throne because His dominion encompasses all. (We) answer the first by denying the suggestion; do you not see that the dominant one does not suggest it, as in His saying: 'And Allah is dominant over His affair' [Yusuf:21]. Yes, perhaps the precedence of those matters is understood from the specificity of the one to whom istīlā' is attributed in a particular matter. And as for the second, the benefit is the indication of the higher by the lower, since it is settled in minds that the Throne is the greatest of creation; so if He dominates it, He dominates everything certainly. This is the opposite of what is well-known of alerting by the lower to the higher, and both are correct. For just as the ruling of the lower is understood for the higher if it is more appropriate, so the opposite is understood if the lower is more appropriate to the ruling. And it was said: It (i.e., istiwā' here) is intention (qasd), and it returns to the attribute of will, like His saying: 'Then He established to the heaven' [Baqara:29], i.e., He intended it. But this is far-fetched because that is transitive with 'ilā' like intention, not with 'alā' like istīlā'. His statement: (Since it is settled in minds etc.) is a repelling of what is said: Perhaps there is a created thing greater than God. His statement: (Since that is transitive with 'ilā' and implication is contrary to the original, so it is not resorted to without necessity.) And al-Shaykh, in one of his two opinions, held that it (i.e., istiwā') is an additional attribute not returning to the previous attributes, even if we do not know it in its essence. But no proof has been established for it, nor is it permissible to rely on the apparent meanings of the verses and hadiths, given the existence of the mentioned probability that it means istīlā' or qasd, albeit weak. So the truth is to withhold judgment while affirming that it is not like the sitting of bodies.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage discusses the attribute of istiwā' (establishing over the Throne). The majority interpretation is that istiwā' means istīlā' (dominion), thus it reduces to the attribute of power. Two objections are raised: (1) istīlā' implies struggle and opposition, which are impossible for God; (2) specifying the Throne is useless since God's dominion is universal. The answers: (1) not all instances of istīlā' imply struggle, as God's absolute dominance does not; (2) the Throne is mentioned to indicate that if He dominates the greatest creation, He dominates all, a form of argument from the greater. A second opinion interprets istiwā' as qasd (intention), returning to will, but this is rejected because the preposition 'alā' is used, while intention takes 'ilā'. A third opinion, attributed to al-Shaykh, holds that istiwā' is an additional unknown attribute, but this lacks proof and relies on literal interpretation of ambiguous texts. The correct stance is to affirm that it is not like bodily sitting.

Key Terms
استواء (istiwā')
استيلاء (istīlā')
قدرة (qudra)
عرش ('arsh)
مجاز (majāz)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that interpreting istiwā' as istīlā' necessarily implies that God overcame a struggling creation.
Correction: The passage clarifies that not all instances of istīlā' imply struggle; God's dominance is absolute and without opposition.

Checkpoint
What is the majority interpretation of istiwā' according to this passage?
A) It is a separate unknown attribute
B) It means intention (qasd)
C) It means dominion (istīlā') and returns to power
D) It means physical sitting

Key Takeaway
The majority view that istiwā' means dominion is rationally defensible and grounded in language.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever resist submitting to God's absolute dominion over your life? What area of your heart are you still trying to 'dominate' despite His overpowering control?
?
When you face doubts about God's attributes, do you insist on human analogies (like struggle and opposition) rather than accepting His transcendence? Ask yourself: what would it cost you to let go of these comparisons?
?
This lesson shows that God's dominion over the Throne indicates His dominion over all. Have you allowed this truth to truly settle in your mind and transform your sense of security in His control?

Alternative Interpretations of Istiwā': Intention and an Unknown Attribute
Al-Jurjani discusses the interpretation of istiwā' as qasd and as an additional attribute, and concludes with the correct approach.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the minority interpretations of istiwā' as intention and as a separate attribute, and why the correct stance is to affirm without literal comparison.

Core Idea
Other interpretations of istiwā' are weak or lack proof; the true position is tawaqquf.

Besides the majority view, some scholars interpreted istiwā' as qasd (intention), linking it to the attribute of will, while others considered it a separate unknown attribute. Al-Jurjani evaluates these views and concludes that the only safe stance is to affirm the reality of istiwā' without comparing it to bodies.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the second and third opinions
Read the Arabic passage that presents the interpretation of istiwā' as qasd and the opinion of al-Shaykh, followed by the conclusion. Then read the translation.
Pay attention to the grammatical argument against the qasd interpretation.
2
Understand why the intention interpretation is rejected
The passage explains that the preposition 'alā' is used with istiwā', while intention requires 'ilā'. This linguistic point makes the interpretation far-fetched.
The principle is that tamīl (implying a different preposition) is not resorted to without necessity.
3
Note the third opinion and its lack of proof
Al-Shaykh's opinion that istiwā' is an additional, unknown attribute is presented but deemed lacking proof. The passage warns against relying on literal meanings of ambiguous texts.
The existence of plausible alternative interpretations (istīlā' or qasd) prevents reliance on apparent meanings.
4
Grasp the final stance: tawaqquf
The correct position is to withhold judgment (tawaqquf) about the exact nature of istiwā', while affirming that it is not like bodies sitting.
This avoids both anthropomorphism and complete rejection of the attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?
The author rejects a plausible opinion (qasd) because of a linguistic technicality. What does this teach you about intellectual honesty? Are you willing to abandon a comforting theological position if it lacks evidence, even if it leaves you with unresolved questions?
?
You claim to affirm Allah's attributes without ta'tīl (denial) or tamthīl (anthropomorphism). But in your private moments, when you think of Allah's mercy, anger, or hand, do you unconsciously borrow images from human experience? What practice will you adopt to purify your belief, so that your worship is directed to the One who is not like anything?

The Attribute of al-Wajh: Metaphor or Distinct Attribute?
Al-Jurjani discusses the attribute of face: whether it is a separate attribute or identical to existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over the divine attribute of 'face' (wajh) and why it must be interpreted metaphorically as referring to Allah's essence and attributes.

Core Idea
The 'face' of Allah is not a limb but a metaphor for His essence and attributes.

This lesson examines the fourth attribute discussed by al-Jurjani: al-wajh (the Face). Two scholarly opinions are presented, one viewing it as a distinct positive attribute and another equating it with existence. Both lack decisive proof, leading to the conclusion that the term must be taken metaphorically. We will explore the reasoning behind this metaphorical interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the two opinions mentioned at the beginning.
2
Identify the two opinions
Identify the difference between the opinion of al-Shaykh (and others) who affirm it as a distinct attribute, and al-Qadi who equates it with existence.
Both opinions are noted as lacking decisive proof.
3
Understand the warning (tanbih)
The warning explains why the literal meaning is impossible and why a metaphorical interpretation is necessary.
4
Reflect on the marginal notes
The marginal notes address a potential problem: if 'face' were a separate attribute, the verse would imply only that attribute remains, which is absurd.
This reinforces the metaphorical interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصفة ( الرابعة : ( الوجه قال تعالى : ويبقى وجه ربك، كل شيء هالك إلا وجهه أثبته الشيخ في أحد قوليه وأبو إسحاق الإسفرايني والسلف صفة) ثبوتية ( زائدة ) على ما مر من الصفات وقال في قول آخر وأوفقه القاضي أنه الوجود ( وهو كما قبله ) أعني الاستواء ( في عدم القاطع وعدم جواز التعويل على الظواهر مع قيام الاحتمال . (تنبيه : الوجه وضع في اللغة ( للجارحة ) المخصوصة حقيقة ولا يجوز إرادتها في حقه تعالى ( ولم يوضع لصفة أخرى ( مجهولة لنا ( بل لا يجوز وضعه لما لا يعقله المخاطب إذ المقصود من الأوضاع تفهيم المعاني ( فتعين المجاز والتجوز به عما يعقل وثبت بالدليل متعين وهو أن يتجوز به عن الذات الصفات فإن الباقي هو ذاته تعالى مع مجموع صفاته وما سواه هالك غير باق . قوله : ( أثبته الشيخ إلخ سوق الكلام يدل على أن الشيخ ومن تبعه جعل الوجه في الآية المذكورة صفة غير الصفات السبع وهو مشكل جداً إذ يكون معنى الآية كل شيء هالك إلا تلك الصفة فيلزم هلاك باقي الصفات بل الذات أيضاً ، لوجوب عموم المستثنى منه فالصواب أن يكون الصفات كما ذكره الشارح . قوله : (بل لا يجوز وضعه إلخ لم لا يجوز أن يعلم النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام الذي هو ) المخاطب ولو سلم فلعل القصد المعلل بعلاء وكبح عنان الذهن كما قيل في المتشابهات وحصر فائدة الوضع في فهم المعنى ممنوع .
The fourth attribute: al-wajh (face). He, exalted, said: 'And there remains the face of your Lord, everything perishes except His face' [Rahman:27]. Al-Shaykh, in one of his two opinions, and Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini, and the salaf affirmed it as a positive attribute, additional to the previously mentioned attributes. And in another opinion, which al-Qadi favored, it is existence (al-wujud). And it is like the previous one, i.e., istiwā', in lacking a decisive proof and the impermissibility of relying on apparent meanings given the existence of probability. (Warning: The word 'wajh' in language is placed for the specific bodily organ as a literal meaning, and it is not permissible to intend that for Him, exalted. And it was not placed for another attribute unknown to us. Rather, it is not permissible to place it for what the addressee cannot conceive, because the purpose of linguistic coinages is to convey meanings. So metaphor is necessary, and using it metaphorically for something intelligible that is established by proof is necessary, which is that it is used metaphorically for the essence and attributes. For what remains is His essence, exalted, with the totality of His attributes, and everything else perishes and does not remain.) His statement: (Al-Shaykh affirmed it etc.) The flow of speech indicates that al-Shaykh and those who followed him made the 'face' in the mentioned verse an attribute other than the seven attributes, which is very problematic because then the meaning of the verse would be: everything perishes except that attribute, which would necessitate the perishing of the remaining attributes, even the essence, due to the necessity of the generality of the excluded matter. Therefore, the correct view is that it is among the attributes as the commentator mentioned. His statement: (Rather, it is not permissible to place it etc.) Why is it not permissible that the Prophet, peace be upon him, who is the addressee, knows it? And even if granted, perhaps the purpose is to incite curiosity and restrain the mind, as is said about the mutashabihat. And restricting the benefit of coinage to understanding the meaning is not admitted.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage discusses the attribute of face (wajh). Two opinions are presented: (1) Al-Shaykh, Abu Ishaq, and the salaf affirm it as a distinct positive attribute additional to the seven attributes. (2) Al-Qadi favors that it means existence (wujud) itself. Both lack decisive proof and rely on probability. The passage then gives a warning: the word 'wajh' literally means a physical face, which is impossible for God. It cannot be used for an unknown meaning because language aims to convey understanding. Therefore, it must be interpreted metaphorically. The correct metaphorical interpretation is that 'face' refers to the essence and attributes of God, as what remains is His essence with all attributes, while everything else perishes. The marginal notes further clarify: if 'face' were a separate attribute, the verse would imply that only that attribute remains, negating other attributes and the essence, which is absurd. Another note questions the premise that it is impermissible to place a word for an unknown meaning, but the reply suggests that such usage might serve to stimulate thought, though the overall position remains that metaphor is required.

Key Terms
وجه (wajh)
وجود (wujud)
مجاز (majāz)
ذات (dhāt)
صفات (sifāt)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some take the word 'face' literally as a physical limb of God.
Correction: The passage clarifies that literal meaning is impossible for God, and it must be interpreted metaphorically as essence and attributes.

Checkpoint
According to the warning (tanbih), what is the correct interpretation of 'wajh' in the verse?
A) It is a physical limb
B) It is a separate unknown attribute
C) It is metaphorical for the essence and attributes
D) It means existence only

Key Takeaway
The divine attribute al-wajh must be understood metaphorically as referring to Allah's essence and attributes, not as a literal face.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite verses like 'every thing perishes except His face', do you find your mind drifting to a physical image despite knowing it's impermissible? How will you actively replace that image with the understanding that it refers to His essence and attributes?
?
The passage warns against relying on apparent meanings when there is probability. In your own study of Allah's names and attributes, how often do you pause to check whether you are settling for a superficial understanding rather than seeking the deeper metaphorical meaning taught by scholars? What specific verse will you re-examine today?
?
Al-Jurjani mentions a possible objection that the Prophet (peace be upon him) might understand an unknown meaning. Yet he insists language is for conveying meaning. When you encounter a difficult verse, do you feel content with 'only Allah knows', or does it drive you to uncover what can be understood metaphorically? What step will you take this week to grow in your understanding of the ambiguous verses?

The Divine Attribute of al-Yad (Hand): Meanings and Interpretations
Al-Jurjani discusses the attribute of hand, presenting three theological positions and the honor argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different scholarly interpretations of the attribute 'hand' (yad) in the Quran, and why the majority view it as a metaphor for power rather than a physical limb.

Core Idea
The 'hand' of Allah is a metaphor for perfect power, not a physical organ.

This passage from al-Jurjani's Sharh al-Mawaqif presents a key debate in Islamic theology: how to understand the Quranic references to Allah's 'hand'. Does it refer to an additional attribute beyond the essence, or is it a metaphorical expression for His power? The discussion involves the views of the early scholars (salaf), the majority of theologians, and the Mu'tazila, each offering a different perspective. By examining the verses and their explanations, we gain insight into the method of interpreting ambiguous attributes (mutashabihat).

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get a clear sense of the discussion.
Pay attention to the key terms like يد, قدرة, مجاز.
2
Identify the three main positions
Find and list the views of al-Shaykh (and the salaf), the majority, and the Mu'tazila. Note how each defines 'hand'.
The majority view is that the two hands are a metaphor for power.
3
Understand the honor argument
Examine why the creation of Adam is specified with 'two hands' and how this is compared to the Ka'ba being called 'My House'.
This shows that specification indicates honor, not a separate power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصفة ( الخامسة : اليد، قال الله تعالى : يد الله فوق أيديهم : ما منعك أن تسجد لما خلقت بيدي فأثبت الشيخ صفتين ثبوتيتين (زائدتين) على الذات وسائر الصفات لكن لا الجارحتين ، ( وعليه السلف وإليه ميل القاضي في بعض كتبه وقال : الأكثر إنهما مجاز عن القدرة فإنه شائع، وخلقته بيدي أي بقدرة (كاملة) ولم يرد بقدرتين ( وتخصيص خلق آدم بذلك ) مع أن الكل مخلوق بقدرته تعالى ( تشريف ) وتكريم له، (كما أضاف الكعبة إلى نفسه في قوله : أن طهرا بيتي للتشريف مع أنه مالك للمخلوقات كلها، ) (و) كما خصص المؤمنين (بالعبودية) لذلك في قوله : إن عبادي ليس لك عليهم سلطان ﴾ [ الحجر : ٤٢] . ( وقالت المعتزلة بأن ( اليد مجاز ( عن القادرية بناء على قوله : (أي بقدرة (كاملة لأن العمل باليدين يشعر بالتكلف فإذا أسند إلى الباري تعالى یراد به الكمال .
The fifth attribute: al-yad (hand). Allah, exalted, said: 'The hand of Allah is above their hands' [Fath:10] and 'What prevented you from prostrating to what I created with My two hands' [Sad:75]. Al-Shaykh affirmed two positive attributes (additional) over the essence and the rest of the attributes, but not as two bodily limbs. And the salaf are upon that, and al-Qadi inclined to it in some of his books. But the majority said: They (the two hands) are a metaphor for power, for that is common. And 'I created him with My two hands' means with (complete) power, and He did not intend two powers. And the specification of the creation of Adam with that, even though everything is created by His power, is an honor and veneration for him, just as He added the Ka'ba to Himself in His saying: 'Purify My House' for honor, while He is the owner of all creation. And just as He specifically honored the believers with servitude in His saying: 'Indeed, My servants, you have no authority over them' [Hijr:42]. And the Mu'tazila said that the hand is a metaphor for ability (al-qadariyya) based on... His statement: (i.e., with (complete) power, because the action with two hands suggests effort, so when attributed to the Exalted, perfection is intended.)
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage discusses the attribute of hand (yad). Al-Shaykh affirms two positive attributes of hand, but not as physical limbs. The majority interprets them as a metaphor for power (qudra), as it is common in Arabic to use 'hand' for power. The specification of Adam with 'two hands' indicates perfect power, not two separate powers. This specification is an honor for Adam, similar to how the Ka'ba is called 'My House'. The Mu'tazila interpret it as a metaphor for ability. The marginal note clarifies that using two hands conveys perfection of power, as working with both hands implies effort, but for God it indicates complete power.

Key Terms
يد (yad)
قدرة (qudra)
مجاز (majāz)
تشريف (tashrīf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hand of Allah is a physical limb like human hands.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the hand is not a physical limb; it is either an additional attribute or a metaphor for power, and the majority interpret it metaphorically.

Checkpoint
According to the majority, what does 'hand' in the verses refer to?
A) Two physical limbs
B) A metaphor for power
C) Two separate additional attributes
D) A metaphor for will

Key Takeaway
The attribute of 'hand' in the Quran is understood by the majority of Muslim scholars as a metaphor for Allah's perfect power, and the dual form indicates honor and perfection, not physical limbs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read 'the hand of Allah is above their hands,' do you truly feel that every act of yours is under His absolute control? How does this affect the way you act when no one is watching?
?
If the dual 'hand' is a sign of honor for Adam, do you live with the awareness that Allah has honored you with existence and guidance? What would change if you truly believed that every ability you have is a gift of His 'hand'?
?
The majority understood 'hand' as power. In your daily life, do you recognize Allah's power in your successes and failures, or do you attribute them to your own effort? What step can you take today to rely more on His power?

Attribute of the Foot (Qadam)
Hadiths about God's foot in Hell and its metaphorical interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the hadith about God placing His foot in Hell must be interpreted metaphorically and what the correct interpretation is.

Core Idea
God's 'foot' is a metaphor for what repels Hell's severity, not a literal limb.

The eighth attribute discussed is the 'foot' (qadam), mentioned in several hadiths about Hell. These texts might seem to imply a bodily part for God, but scholars insist on metaphorical interpretation (ta'wil) to preserve divine transcendence. This lesson examines the hadiths, rejects a flawed interpretation, and presents the accepted metaphorical meaning.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadiths about the foot
Read the Arabic passage containing the various narrations about God placing His foot in Hell. Note the different phrasings.
The hadiths use terms 'qadam' and 'rijl'.
2
Identify the problem of literalism
Recognize that taking these words literally would attribute a body part to God, which contradicts His transcendence.
This is the key issue that necessitates interpretation.
3
Understand the rejected interpretation
The author mentions al-Āmidī's interpretation (explaining 'al-jabbār' as the keeper of Hell) and dismisses it as invalid.
The rejection is strong: 'mimmā lā yultafatu ilayhi'.
4
Learn the correct metaphorical meaning
The accepted ta'wil is that 'foot' and 'leg' are metaphors for what repels Hell's severity, calms its heat, and cuts off its anguish.
This comes from the work al-Muẓhir.
5
Reflect on the method
Consider why the author provides this interpretation and how it balances the literal text with divine transcendence.
This is a standard approach in kalam for anthropomorphic expressions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصفة الثامنة القدم قال عليه السلام في أثناء حديث مطول فيضع الجبار قدمه في النار فتقول: قط قط، أي حسبي حسبي وفي رواية أخرى حتى يضع رب العزة فيها قدمه فينزوي بعضها إلى بعض وتقول: قط قط بعزتك وكرمك، وفي أخرى يقال لجهنم: هل امتلات؟ وتقول: هل من مزيد حتى يضع الرب قدمه عليها فتقول: قط قط، وتأويل الجبار بمالك خازن النار أو بمن يرفع نفسه عن امتثال التكاليف مما لا يلتفت إليه كيف، وقد ورد في رواية أنس في أثناء حديث: وأما النار فلا تمتلئ حتى يضع الله رجله فيها. قوله: (مما لا يلتفت إليه أبطل تأويل الآمدي ولم يذكر تأويلاً صحيحاً كما ذكر في أمثاله، قيل: وضع القدم والرجل من باب الاتساع والمجاز لم يرد بهما أعيانهما بل أراد بذلك ما يدفع شدتها ويسكن سورتها ويقطع مساءتها كذا في المظهر. الصفة التاسعة: الأصبع قال عليه السلام إن قلب المؤمن بين أصبعين من أصابع الرحمن وفي رواية إن قلوب بني آدم كلها بين أصبعين من أصابع الرحمن كقلب واحد يصرفها كيف يشاء. ولا يمكن إثبات الجارحة وأما وجه التأويل فكما في اليدين. الصفة العاشرة: اليمين قال تعالى: {والسماوات مطويات بيمينه} [الزمر: 67]، وتأويلها بالقدرة التامة ظاهرة.
The eighth attribute: eternity (qidam). He (peace be upon him) said in the course of a long hadith: 'Then the tyrant (al-jabbār) places his foot (qadamahu) in the Fire, and it says: 'Qaṭ, qaṭ', i.e., enough, enough.' And in another narration: 'Until the Lord of Might places His foot (qadamahu) in it, and it contracts part to part and says: 'Qaṭ, qaṭ by Your might and generosity.' And in another: It is said to Hell: 'Have you become full?' And it says: 'Is there any more?' until the Lord places His foot (qadamahu) upon it, and it says: 'Qaṭ, qaṭ.' And interpreting 'al-jabbār' as the owner/keeper of Hell or as one who raises himself from obeying obligations is not to be heeded—how? And it has come in the narration of Anas during a hadith: 'As for the Fire, it will not be filled until Allah places His leg (rijlahu) in it.' His statement: 'not to be heeded' invalidates al-Āmidī's interpretation and he did not mention a correct interpretation as he mentioned in its like. It is said: The placing of the foot and leg is from the scope of extension and metaphor; by them their actual entities are not meant, but rather by that is meant what repels its severity, calms its heat, and cuts off its anguish—as in al-Muẓhir. The ninth attribute: the finger (al-uṣbuʿ). He (peace be upon him) said: 'Indeed, the heart of the believer is between two fingers of the fingers of the Most Merciful.' And in a narration: 'Indeed, the hearts of the children of Adam, all of them, are between two fingers of the fingers of the Most Merciful, like one heart; He turns them as He wills.' And affirming a bodily organ is not possible. As for the mode of interpretation, it is as in the two hands. The tenth attribute: the right hand (al-yamīn). He, exalted, said: {And the heavens will be folded in His right hand} [az-Zumar: 67]. And its interpretation by absolute power is apparent.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage lists three divine attributes—قدم (eternity, but here referring to the hadith about the foot), إصبع (finger), and يمين (right hand)—and addresses the problem of anthropomorphic expressions in transmitted texts. The author cites hadiths where God is described as placing a foot in Hell and as having fingers and a right hand. He then provides interpretations (ta'wil) to avoid affirming a literal body. For the foot, he rejects a specific interpretation by al-Āmidī and mentions that the correct interpretation is metaphorical: the 'foot' means that which repels the Fire. For the finger, he states that affirming a bodily organ is impossible and refers back to the interpretation used for the two hands. For the right hand, he gives the obvious interpretation of absolute power (qudra tāmma). The commentary explains each attribute in order, showing the method of figurative interpretation.

Key Terms
قدم
رجل
الجبار
تأويل
مجاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith about God placing His foot in Hell implies that God has a literal foot or leg.
Correction: The term is metaphorical, meaning what repels the Fire's severity, not a literal body part.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, how should the 'foot' (qadam) in the hadith about Hell be understood?
A) As a literal foot of God
B) As a metaphor for what repels the Fire's severity
C) As the foot of the angel Malik
D) As a reference to God's power only

Key Takeaway
The 'foot' attributed to God in hadith is a metaphor for the force that repels Hell, not a literal limb.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear the hadith about God placing His foot in Hell, does your heart tremble at the severity of the Fire—or do you rationalize it away? How can you let this warning deepen your repentance today?
?
The author rejects literal interpretations to protect God's transcendence. Is there any attribute or description of God that you tend to imagine in human terms? What step will you take this week to purify your conception of Allah?
?
If the 'foot' represents what repels Hell's severity, what deeds or habits in your life are actually fueling the Fire? What specific action can you take today to become a cause for Hell to say 'enough' for you?

Attribute of the Finger (Uṣbuʿ)
Hadith about God's fingers and its metaphorical interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the attribute of the finger (uṣbuʿ) is interpreted metaphorically, following the same method as the interpretation of the hands.

Core Idea
God's fingers are metaphorical, not literal organs, as with the hands.

The ninth attribute is the 'finger' (uṣbuʿ), mentioned in hadiths about the hearts of believers being between two fingers of the Most Merciful. The author notes that affirming a bodily organ is impossible and refers back to the interpretation used for the two hands. This lesson explores the hadith and its metaphorical interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith about the finger
Read the hadith stating that the heart of the believer is between two fingers of the Most Merciful.
There are two versions: one mentioning a believer's heart, another mentioning all hearts.
2
Recognize the literal problem
Understand that a literal reading would attribute a bodily organ (finger) to God, which is impossible (lā yumkinu ithbāt al-jāriḥa).
This is the same issue as with other anthropomorphic attributes.
3
Connect to the interpretation of hands
The author says the mode of interpretation is 'as in the two hands', meaning the same metaphorical method applies.
The interpretation for 'yad' (hand) was likely discussed earlier in the text.
4
Apply the general principle
Like the foot, the finger is a metaphor for God's control and turning of hearts, not a literal digit.
The emphasis is on God's absolute power over hearts.

Attribute of the Right Hand (Yamīn)
Quranic verse about God's right hand and its interpretation as power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the 'right hand' (yamīn) in the Quran is interpreted as God's absolute power (qudra tāmma).

Core Idea
God's right hand is His absolute power, not a literal hand.

The tenth attribute is the 'right hand' (yamīn), mentioned in the Quranic verse 'the heavens will be folded in His right hand'. The author states that its interpretation as absolute power is apparent. This lesson confirms the straightforward metaphorical understanding of this attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic verse
Read the verse from Surah az-Zumar (39:67) that mentions the heavens being folded in His right hand.
The Arabic says 'biyamīnihi'.
2
Recognize the literal problem
A literal right hand would imply a bodily organ, which is incompatible with God's transcendence.
This is the same issue as with other attributes.
3
Accept the interpretation
The author gives the interpretation as 'absolute power' (qudra tāmma) and says it is apparent (ẓāhira).
This interpretation is straightforward and widely accepted.
4
Reflect on consistency
Notice how the same method of ta'wil is applied to all anthropomorphic expressions: they are interpreted as metaphors for God's attributes or actions.
Consistency is key in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصفة الثامنة القدم قال عليه السلام في أثناء حديث مطول فيضع الجبار قدمه في النار فتقول: قط قط، أي حسبي حسبي وفي رواية أخرى حتى يضع رب العزة فيها قدمه فينزوي بعضها إلى بعض وتقول: قط قط بعزتك وكرمك، وفي أخرى يقال لجهنم: هل امتلات؟ وتقول: هل من مزيد حتى يضع الرب قدمه عليها فتقول: قط قط، وتأويل الجبار بمالك خازن النار أو بمن يرفع نفسه عن امتثال التكاليف مما لا يلتفت إليه كيف، وقد ورد في رواية أنس في أثناء حديث: وأما النار فلا تمتلئ حتى يضع الله رجله فيها. قوله: (مما لا يلتفت إليه أبطل تأويل الآمدي ولم يذكر تأويلاً صحيحاً كما ذكر في أمثاله، قيل: وضع القدم والرجل من باب الاتساع والمجاز لم يرد بهما أعيانهما بل أراد بذلك ما يدفع شدتها ويسكن سورتها ويقطع مساءتها كذا في المظهر. الصفة التاسعة: الأصبع قال عليه السلام إن قلب المؤمن بين أصبعين من أصابع الرحمن وفي رواية إن قلوب بني آدم كلها بين أصبعين من أصابع الرحمن كقلب واحد يصرفها كيف يشاء. ولا يمكن إثبات الجارحة وأما وجه التأويل فكما في اليدين. الصفة العاشرة: اليمين قال تعالى: {والسماوات مطويات بيمينه} [الزمر: 67]، وتأويلها بالقدرة التامة ظاهرة.
The eighth attribute: eternity (qidam). He (peace be upon him) said in the course of a long hadith: 'Then the tyrant (al-jabbār) places his foot (qadamahu) in the Fire, and it says: 'Qaṭ, qaṭ', i.e., enough, enough.' And in another narration: 'Until the Lord of Might places His foot (qadamahu) in it, and it contracts part to part and says: 'Qaṭ, qaṭ by Your might and generosity.' And in another: It is said to Hell: 'Have you become full?' And it says: 'Is there any more?' until the Lord places His foot (qadamahu) upon it, and it says: 'Qaṭ, qaṭ.' And interpreting 'al-jabbār' as the owner/keeper of Hell or as one who raises himself from obeying obligations is not to be heeded—how? And it has come in the narration of Anas during a hadith: 'As for the Fire, it will not be filled until Allah places His leg (rijlahu) in it.' His statement: 'not to be heeded' invalidates al-Āmidī's interpretation and he did not mention a correct interpretation as he mentioned in its like. It is said: The placing of the foot and leg is from the scope of extension and metaphor; by them their actual entities are not meant, but rather by that is meant what repels its severity, calms its heat, and cuts off its anguish—as in al-Muẓhir. The ninth attribute: the finger (al-uṣbuʿ). He (peace be upon him) said: 'Indeed, the heart of the believer is between two fingers of the fingers of the Most Merciful.' And in a narration: 'Indeed, the hearts of the children of Adam, all of them, are between two fingers of the fingers of the Most Merciful, like one heart; He turns them as He wills.' And affirming a bodily organ is not possible. As for the mode of interpretation, it is as in the two hands. The tenth attribute: the right hand (al-yamīn). He, exalted, said: {And the heavens will be folded in His right hand} [az-Zumar: 67]. And its interpretation by absolute power is apparent.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage lists three divine attributes—قدم (eternity, but here referring to the hadith about the foot), إصبع (finger), and يمين (right hand)—and addresses the problem of anthropomorphic expressions in transmitted texts. The author cites hadiths where God is described as placing a foot in Hell and as having fingers and a right hand. He then provides interpretations (ta'wil) to avoid affirming a literal body. For the foot, he rejects a specific interpretation by al-Āmidī and mentions that the correct interpretation is metaphorical: the 'foot' means that which repels the Fire. For the finger, he states that affirming a bodily organ is impossible and refers back to the interpretation used for the two hands. For the right hand, he gives the obvious interpretation of absolute power (qudra tāmma). The commentary explains each attribute in order, showing the method of figurative interpretation.

Misconception: The verse implies that God has a literal right hand like a human.
Correction: The 'right hand' is a metaphor for God's absolute power (qudra tāmma).

Key Takeaway

The Hanafi Claim of التكوين as an Additional Attribute
States the Hanafi view that التكوين is an extra attribute beyond the seven.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Hanafi argument for التكوين as an additional attribute and the Ashʿarī response that power suffices with will.

Core Idea
The debate over التكوين hinges on whether power alone can bring about existence or needs a separate attribute.

This passage presents a nuanced theological debate between the Hanafis and Ashʿarīs about the attribute of التكوين (bringing into existence). The Hanafis argue that power (qudra) only bestows validity, not actual existence, so a distinct attribute is required. The Ashʿarī response clarifies that power's effect is existence itself, with will as the specifier. Understanding this exchange clarifies the precise relationship between divine power, will, and creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own dialectical rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the parentheses; they indicate glosses that clarify the argument.
2
Identify the Hanafi claim and its proof
Find where the Hanafis state that التكوين is an additional attribute. What is their proof from the Qur'an? How do they distinguish it from power?
They argue that power only gives validity (ṣiḥḥa), not actual coming into being (kawn).
3
Understand the Ashʿarī response
The response argues that validity is intrinsic possibility, so power's effect is actually the coming into being of the possible object. See how this refutes the need for a separate attribute.
The response uses the concept of imkān dhātī (intrinsic possibility).
4
Follow the objection and final reply
An objection reinterprets 'validity' as the agent's ability to effect. The reply then shows that power makes both action and omission possible, and will specifies one side. No separate attribute is needed.
This final step shows the Ashʿarī reliance on will as the specifier.
5
Summarize the key difference
In one sentence, state the fundamental disagreement: does power directly cause existence or only make it possible?
The Hanafi position requires a distinct attribute; the Ashʿarī position does not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصفة الحادية عشر: التكوين أثبته الحنفية صفة زائدة على السبع المشهورة أخذاً من قوله تعالى: {كن فيكون} [النحل: 40]، فقد جعل قوله: {كن} [النحل: 40] متقدماً على كون الحادثات، أعني وجودها، والمراد به التكوين والإيجاد والتخليق. قالوا: وإنه غير القدرة لأن القدرة أثرها الصحة والصحة لا تستلزم الكون، فلا يكون الكون أثراً لقدرة وأثر التكوين هو الكون. (الجواب: إن الصحة الإمكان وأنه للممكن ذاتي فلا يصلح أثراً للقدرة) لأن ما بالذات لا يعلل بالغير بل به أي بإمكان الشيء في نفسه تعلل المقدورية فيقال: مقدور لأنه ممكن وذلك غير مقدور لأنه واجب أو ممتنع، فإذن أثر القدرة هو الكون ( أي كون المقدور وجوده لا صحته وإمكانه ) فاستغنى عن) إثبات (صفة) أخرى ( كذلك ) أي يكون أثرها الكون ( فإن قيل: المراد بالصحة) التي جعلناها أثراً للقدرة هو (صحة الفعل بمعنى التأثير والإيجاد من الفاعل ( لا صحة المفعول في نفسه)، وهذه الصحة إمكانه الذاتي الذي لا يمكن تعليله بغيره )، وأما الصحة الأولى فهي بالقياس إلى الفاعل ومعللة بالقدرة ) فإن القدرة هي الصفة التي باعتبارها يصح من الفاعل طرفا الفعل والترك ) على سواء من الشيء المقدور له، ( فلا يحصل بها ) منه ( أحدهما بعينه بل لا بد في حصوله من صفة أخرى متعلقة به أي بذلك الطرف وحده فتلك الصفة هي التكوين : قلنا : كل منهما ) أي من ذينك الطرفين ( يصلح أثراً لها أي للقدرة ( وإنما يحتاج صدور أحدهما) بعينه عنها (إلى مخصص ) بعينه ( وهو الإرادة ( المتعلقة بذلك الطرف، (و) حينئذ ( لا حاجة إلى مبدأ للكون غير القدرة ) المؤثرة فيه بواسطة الإرادة المتعلقة به
The eleventh attribute: bringing into existence (al-takwīn). The Hanafis affirmed it as an additional attribute beyond the seven well-known ones, taken from His saying exalted: {Be, and it is} [an-Naḥl: 40]. For He made His saying: {Be} [an-Naḥl: 40] prior to the coming into being (kawn) of originated things, meaning their existence, and by it is meant bringing into existence (al-takwīn), causing to be (al-ījād), and creating (al-takhlīq). They said: And it is other than power (al-qudra) because the effect of power is validity (al-ṣiḥḥa), and validity does not entail coming into being (al-kawn), so coming into being is not an effect of power, while the effect of bringing into existence (al-takwīn) is coming into being. (The answer: Validity is possibility (al-imkān), and it is intrinsic to the possible, so it cannot serve as an effect of power) because what is intrinsic is not caused by another; rather, by it—i.e., by the possibility of the thing in itself—the potential to be an object of power (al-maqdūriyya) is caused, so it is said: it is an object of power because it is possible, and that is not an object of power because it is necessary or impossible. Therefore, the effect of power is coming into being (i.e., the coming into being of the object of power, i.e., its existence, not its validity and possibility). So there is no need) to affirm (another attribute) similarly (i.e., whose effect is coming into being). For it is said: What is meant by 'validity' which we made the effect of power is (the validity of the act in the sense of the effect and bringing into existence from the agent, not the validity of the object in itself), and this validity is its intrinsic possibility which cannot be caused by another. As for the first validity, it is relative to the agent and caused by power). For power is the attribute by virtue of which the two sides of action and omission are valid from the agent) equally for the thing that is an object of power for him, (so neither of them occurs) from him (specifically without another attribute related to it—i.e., to that side alone—and that attribute is bringing into existence). We said: Each of them (i.e., of those two sides) is suitable as an effect of it—i.e., of power. (But the occurrence of one of them specifically from it requires a specific specifier, which is the will) related to that side, (and) then (there is no need for a principle of coming into being other than power) which effects it through the mediation of the will related to it.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi doctrine that التكوين (bringing into existence) is an additional attribute beyond the seven essential attributes of God. The passage is a complex argument that includes an initial claim, a response, and a further objection. The Hanafis argue that power (قدرة) only gives the validity of an act (صحة الفعل), not the actual coming into being (كون), so a separate attribute of التكوين is needed. The response (likely from an Ashʿarī perspective) counters that validity is intrinsic possibility (إمكان ذاتي) and thus cannot be an effect of power; rather, the effect of power is the coming into being of the possible object. Then an objection is raised: the 'validity' meant is the agent's ability to effect and bring into existence, which is caused by power. The reply is that power makes both sides of action and omission equally possible, but to actualize one side requires a specifier (will), and no separate attribute of تكوين is needed. The argument proceeds step by step through the dialectic.

Key Terms

القدرة
الصحة
الإمكان

الإرادة
المقدورية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'validity' (ṣiḥḥa) has a single, unambiguous meaning in the debate about divine power and creation.
Correction: The passage distinguishes between two meanings of ṣiḥḥa: (a) the agent's ability to effect (which is caused by power) and (b) the intrinsic possibility of the object (which is not caused). This distinction is crucial to the argument.

Checkpoint
What do the Hanafis claim about the attribute of التكوين?
A) It is the same as power (قدرة)
B) It is an additional attribute beyond the seven well-known ones
C) It is identical to will (إرادة)
D) It is a metaphorical expression

Key Takeaway
The debate over التكوين ultimately shows that according to the Ashʿarīs, power (qudra) together with will (irāda) is sufficient for creation, without needing a separate attribute of bringing into existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'God created everything,' do you truly believe that His power and will are sufficient, or do you sometimes imagine a separate 'making' step? How would your tawakkul change if you fully internalized the Ashʿarī view that nothing is needed beyond His will?
?
Reflect on a recent event you attributed to a secondary cause (e.g., your effort, luck). Can you trace its ultimate origin solely to God's power and will? If not, what prevents you from doing so?
?
The Hanafis argued that power only gives validity, not existence. Do you ever act as if God's power is 'automatic'—that things must happen because He can? How does insisting on the role of will (irāda) deepen your reliance on God in this moment?

The Objection that Power Needs an Additional Attribute for Actualization
Objection and reply on the necessity of al-takwīn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that power alone cannot produce a specific act without an additional attribute, and the reply that power plus will is sufficient.

Core Idea
Power plus will, not a separate attribute, suffices for specific acts.

This passage continues the debate on whether al-takwīn is a distinct attribute. An opponent argues that power only makes both action and omission equally possible, so actualizing one side requires an additional attribute. The response clarifies that the specifier (will) is sufficient, eliminating the need for al-takwīn.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objection starts with 'فإن قيل' and claims that the validity caused by power is only the validity of the act from the agent, not the object's intrinsic possibility. It argues that power alone cannot specify one side, so a separate attribute (al-takwīn) is needed.
Notice the distinction between two kinds of validity.
2
Study the reply
The reply ('قلنا') states that each side is already a potential effect of power. What specifies one side is the will (al-irāda) related to that side, not a third attribute.
The key point: the specifier is will, not al-takwīn.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that there is no need for a principle of coming-into-being (al-takwīn) beyond power mediated by will.
This directly addresses the original debate.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: المراد بالصحة التي جعلناها أثراً للقدرة هو صحة الفعل بمعنى التأثير والإيجاد من الفاعل لا صحة المفعول في نفسه، وهذه الصحة إمكانه الذاتي الذي لا يمكن تعليله بغيره، وأما الصحة الأولى فهي بالقياس إلى الفاعل ومعللة بالقدرة فإن القدرة هي الصفة التي باعتبارها يصح من الفاعل طرفا الفعل والترك على سواء من الشيء المقدور له، فلا يحصل بها منه أحدهما بعينه بل لا بد في حصوله من صفة أخرى متعلقة به أي بذلك الطرف وحده فتلك الصفة هي التكوين : قلنا : كل منهما أي من ذينك الطرفين يصلح أثراً لها أي للقدرة وإنما يحتاج صدور أحدهما بعينه عنها إلى مخصص بعينه وهو الإرادة المتعلقة بذلك الطرف، وحينئذ لا حاجة إلى مبدأ للكون غير القدرة المؤثرة فيه بواسطة الإرادة المتعلقة به. قوله: (وحينئذ لا حاجة إلى مبدأ إلخ) اعترض عليه بأن التكوين هو المعنى الذي نجده في الفاعل وبه يمتاز عن غيره ويرتبط بالمفعول، وإن لم يوجد بعد وهذا لمعنى يعم الموجب أيضاً، بل هو موجود في الواجب بالنسبة إلى نفس القدرة والإرادة فكيف لا يكون صفة أخرى. وأجيب : بأن الظاهر أن ارتباط الفاعل بالمفعول صلاحية تأثيره فيه والمعنى الذي يخص الفاعل مبدأ تلك
For if it is said: What is meant by 'validity' which we made the effect of power is the validity of the act in the sense of the effect and bringing into existence from the agent, not the validity of the object in itself, and this validity is its intrinsic possibility which cannot be caused by another. As for the first validity, it is relative to the agent and caused by power. For power is the attribute by virtue of which the two sides of action and omission are valid from the agent equally of the thing that is an object of power for him, so neither of them occurs from him specifically without another attribute related to it—i.e., to that side alone—and that attribute is bringing into existence (al-takwīn). We said: Each of them—i.e., of those two sides—is suitable as an effect of it, i.e., of power. But the occurrence of one of them specifically from it requires a specific specifier, which is the will related to that side. And so there is no need for a principle of coming into being other than power which effects it through the mediation of the will related to it. His statement: ('And so there is no need for a principle etc.') It was objected to it that bringing into existence (al-takwīn) is the meaning which we find in the agent and by which he is distinguished from others and is connected to the object, even if it does not yet exist, and this meaning also applies to the necessitating agent; rather, it exists in the Necessary Being in relation to power and will themselves, so how is it not another attribute? And it was answered: It appears that the connection of the agent to the object is the suitability of his effect in it, and the meaning that is specific to the agent is the principle of that... Commentary
This passage continues the debate on whether التكوين is a distinct attribute. It presents an objection: the 'validity' in question is the agent's ability to effect and bring into existence, which is caused by power. But power only makes both action and omission equally possible; to actualize one side requires an additional attribute related to that side alone, which is التكوين. The reply is that each side is already a possible effect of power; the specific occurrence of one side requires only a specifier (will), not a separate attribute. Then a further objection notes that التكوين is a meaning found in the agent that connects him to the object, even before the object exists; this meaning is found even in the Necessary Being in relation to power and will, so it must be a distinct attribute. The answer is that the connection is merely the suitability of the agent's effect, not an extra attribute. The text is cut off, but the argument is clear.

The Objection that al-Takwīn is a Distinct Attribute in the Agent
Second objection and partial answer on the distinctness of al-takwīn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that al-takwīn is a distinct attribute because it is a meaning found in the agent that connects him to the object, and the incomplete answer that this connection is merely the suitability of effect.

Core Idea
The connection between agent and object is suitability, not an extra attribute.

After the previous reply, a new objection arises: al-takwīn seems to be a real meaning in the agent that distinguishes him and links him to the object, even before the object exists. This meaning is also found in the Necessary Being in relation to power and will. The answer, though cut off, suggests that this connection is merely the suitability of the agent's effect, not a separate attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the second objection
The objection claims that al-takwīn is a meaning we find in the agent, by which he is distinguished and which connects him to the object, even if the object does not yet exist. It also applies to the necessitating agent and even to God in relation to His own power and will.
This objection tries to prove that al-takwīn is a distinct attribute.
2
Examine the answer
The answer begins by stating that the connection is just the suitability of the agent's effect. The text cuts off, but the intended answer is that no extra attribute is needed.
The answer is incomplete, but the direction is clear.
3
Reflect on the overall debate
The passage shows the back-and-forth between those who argue for al-takwīn as a separate attribute and those who reduce it to power and will.
This debate is part of a larger discussion on divine attributes.

فإن قيل: المراد بالصحة التي جعلناها أثراً للقدرة هو صحة الفعل بمعنى التأثير والإيجاد من الفاعل لا صحة المفعول في نفسه، وهذه الصحة إمكانه الذاتي الذي لا يمكن تعليله بغيره، وأما الصحة الأولى فهي بالقياس إلى الفاعل ومعللة بالقدرة فإن القدرة هي الصفة التي باعتبارها يصح من الفاعل طرفا الفعل والترك على سواء من الشيء المقدور له، فلا يحصل بها منه أحدهما بعينه بل لا بد في حصوله من صفة أخرى متعلقة به أي بذلك الطرف وحده فتلك الصفة هي التكوين : قلنا : كل منهما أي من ذينك الطرفين يصلح أثراً لها أي للقدرة وإنما يحتاج صدور أحدهما بعينه عنها إلى مخصص بعينه وهو الإرادة المتعلقة بذلك الطرف، وحينئذ لا حاجة إلى مبدأ للكون غير القدرة المؤثرة فيه بواسطة الإرادة المتعلقة به. قوله: (وحينئذ لا حاجة إلى مبدأ إلخ) اعترض عليه بأن التكوين هو المعنى الذي نجده في الفاعل وبه يمتاز عن غيره ويرتبط بالمفعول، وإن لم يوجد بعد وهذا لمعنى يعم الموجب أيضاً، بل هو موجود في الواجب بالنسبة إلى نفس القدرة والإرادة فكيف لا يكون صفة أخرى. وأجيب : بأن الظاهر أن ارتباط الفاعل بالمفعول صلاحية تأثيره فيه والمعنى الذي يخص الفاعل مبدأ تلك
For if it is said: What is meant by 'validity' which we made the effect of power is the validity of the act in the sense of the effect and bringing into existence from the agent, not the validity of the object in itself, and this validity is its intrinsic possibility which cannot be caused by another. As for the first validity, it is relative to the agent and caused by power. For power is the attribute by virtue of which the two sides of action and omission are valid from the agent equally of the thing that is an object of power for him, so neither of them occurs from him specifically without another attribute related to it—i.e., to that side alone—and that attribute is bringing into existence (al-takwīn). We said: Each of them—i.e., of those two sides—is suitable as an effect of it, i.e., of power. But the occurrence of one of them specifically from it requires a specific specifier, which is the will related to that side. And so there is no need for a principle of coming into being other than power which effects it through the mediation of the will related to it. His statement: ('And so there is no need for a principle etc.') It was objected to it that bringing into existence (al-takwīn) is the meaning which we find in the agent and by which he is distinguished from others and is connected to the object, even if it does not yet exist, and this meaning also applies to the necessitating agent; rather, it exists in the Necessary Being in relation to power and will themselves, so how is it not another attribute? And it was answered: It appears that the connection of the agent to the object is the suitability of his effect in it, and the meaning that is specific to the agent is the principle of that... [text incomplete]
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage continues the debate on whether التكوين is a distinct attribute. It presents an objection: the 'validity' in question is the agent's ability to effect and bring into existence, which is caused by power. But power only makes both action and omission equally possible; to actualize one side requires an additional attribute related to that side alone, which is التكوين. The reply is that each side is already a possible effect of power; the specific occurrence of one side requires only a specifier (will), not a separate attribute. Then a further objection notes that التكوين is a meaning found in the agent that connects him to the object, even before the object exists; this meaning is found even in the Necessary Being in relation to power and will, so it must be a distinct attribute. The answer is that the connection is merely the suitability of the agent's effect, not an extra attribute. The text is cut off, but the argument is clear.

Key Terms
التكوين
القدرة
الإرادة
المفعول
الموجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the objection claim about al-takwīn?
A) It is merely a relation
B) It is a meaning found in the agent that distinguishes him and connects him to the object
C) It is identical to power
D) It only applies to God

Key Takeaway
The objection that al-takwīn is a distinct attribute because it connects agent to object is countered by the idea that this connection is merely the suitability of effect.
